SPECIALIST for the Treatment of Nervous
Complaints, Eiabetes, Tumors, &c.
JUgistered by the Medical Bo tv lof N.S. W. Lite House Sur-
geon t > the Great Northern Hospital. L'mfa %
I am aware that I am not strictly following the paths
of professional etiquette by advertising myself a-; a
Specialist for the t’ eatnvnt of this class of disease, but,
unfortunately for the suffering public in Australia, the
apathy shown by Qualified Men in the treatment of
Chronic and Nervous Complaints has induced men of the
lowest grade of intellect (whose only qualifications are
unlimited assurance) to spring up, and by specious
advertisements and false promises trap the unwary.
It is very evident that sufferers from these diseases
have been scandalously treated by these unqualified
persons The evidence taken at the Medical Enquiry
held in Sydney (published in the Daily Telegraph, Nov.
6th 7th and 6th. 1800) plainly shows the disgraceful
manner in which patients are duped. Not only are
they robbed of their money, but what is of infinitely
greater importance, their health is injured also. It is
therefore time that Legally Qualified Men entered the
field to fight these pests of society with their own
weapons, viz., Advertising.
It is utterly impossible for those who are suffering
from* disease 5, who are scattered over nil parts of
Australasia, to ascertain which Qualified Practitioner
makes these diseases his speciality unless he advertises.
I consider therefore, that by advertising I not only
strike a blow at the Quackery which now exists, but I also add
to the usefulness of my profession and in no way lessen my
personal dignity .
V.y Qualiflcations I invite the fullest investigation into. For some cor.siderabl# time I was House Surgeon to the
Great Northern Hospital, London, where I had special opportunities of ot.serving the many phases of the Nervous and
texua' System, and to be ome acquainted with the most scientific methods of effecting their cure.
My sole attention is given to the treatment of these diseases, and when a person consults me he is informed at the
commencement whether I can effect his cure or not. The patient is not deluded with false and specious promises, and
if I undertake the case
I Absolutely Guarantee a Complete and Permanent Cure.
My great success in the treatment of these diseases enables me to give this guarantee without fear.

SATURDAY, JUNE 6, 1891.
The Increase of Population.
It is an accepted axiom of modern philosophy
that the world is, rapidly filling up, and by-
and-bye it will grow to be too full. Pro-
found, hard-thinking, fieshless men of know-
ledge are even beginning to calculate the
exact year, or the exact decade, when the
human race will commence to be hungry
because the earth will no longer grow enough
provender to feed its inhabitants, and at the
present rate of increase it is calculated, with
tolerable confidence, that less than two
centuries will find man in an exceedingly
tight place. In almost every country where
census returns are available the population
is rapidly increasing, and as the increase of
food is limited, while that of humanity seems
to be practically limitable only by the earth’s
capacity for sustaining life, it would appear
as if something of a disastrous character
must ensue sooner or later. The problem
is an altogether new one—so far as history
can show—even upon this old, old, un-
speakably ancient earth, for hitherto the
increase of the human race seems to
have been kept down by local famines, or
plague, or want of sanitation, or by the use
of judicious violence. Now and then, in
ancient times, a c'vilised race, such as that
of Egypt, has commenced to multiply in
some corner of the earth in a way that
threatened to tax the resources of the soil,
but somewhere, out of space and geo-
graphical darkness, there always issued
a bloodthirsty rival nation, which settled the
problem by wholesale massacre, just when
it threatened to become interesting. The
world never had a chance to decide what
course it would adopt when it became too
much crowded to feed its inhabitants, for
some obtrusive Hun or Scythian inevitably
interfered ; or else some new distemper, for
which science could find no remedy, post-
poned the question for another ten centuries;
or, failing that, the nations rose and banged
each other about for thirty years on some
obscure point of doctrine. But sooner or
later the problem must come up for settle-
ment. The Huns and Scythians float loans
in Europe now, and desolate the country
with debentures instead of with fire and
sword. The plague and the Black Death
seem to be approaching extinction as sanitary
science advances ; and very few nations care
enough for their creeds and gospels to con-
duct religious wars with anything like their
old vigour. And, in any case, war is not
what it used to be, for the mediaeval
European who clubbed and speared his
enemy at close quarters produced far better
returns in the way of corpses than his
descendant with the breechloader and the
torpedo, and even the dynamite gun doesn’t
thin out the inhabitants half so effectually as
a good many older weapons seem to have
done. Moreover, war has grown so expen-
sive that each corpse often costs as much as
£lOO, whereas in the old days of cheap homi-
cide the clothes of the deceased more than
paid for killing him. Amid all these novel
conditions, therefore, it is reasonable to
suppose that population is increasing faster
than it ever did before, and the fact that the
world has never yet been over-populated is
no proof that it will not be over-populated
in the early future.
* # «
It is an ominous foreshadowing of this
future trouble that most of the great nations
of the world are already beginning to find a
difficulty in disposing of their “ surplus
population.” In other words, the old, time-
honoured expedient of emigration is begin-
ning to be played out. It was a beautiful
and simple device while it lasted, and it has
done good service for several centuries.
Many a great national disaster has been
averted in times gone past by the handy
expedient of finding a new country, and
extirpating the inhabitants, and then using
the empty land as a receptacle for the over-
flow of Europe or Asia, but unluckily the
supply of new countries is limited, and only a
certain class of inhabitants are suited for
extirpation. It has been estimated that as
many as sixty million human beings Avere
wiped out by various devices in America to
make more elbow-room for the European,
and but for that holocaust both America and
Europe might by this time have been in dire
straits through the superabundance of their
population. Such an experiment, hoAvever,
cannot Avell be repeated, and nearly all the
nations which Avere weak and humble enough
to allow themselves to be exterminated in
order to make room for the armed Christian
have already been demolished, so that this
resource is practically at an end. Those
races Avhich still possess a considerable
expanse of empty territory are iioav begin-
ning to understand that its very emptiness is
a thing to be prized, and instead of welcom-
ing the cast-off population of every country
on earth they are anxious to preserve their
superfluous land to accommodate their own
natural increase. Failing this, they may
groAv a nation and have no place to put
it, for it is becoming more and more
evident that land is scarce and the
human race is exceedingly plentiful. The
Avorld is as large as it ever Avas, yet it is
difficult to find standing-room for the half-
million IlebreAvs avlio are now making their
exit from Russia, and though it is an acknow-
ledged fact that a Hebrew must stand some-
where, yet it is more than doubtful if a
civilised country can afford to take in
500,000 emigrants in the present state of
society. In fact, leaving out those regions
Avhich are—from various causes—uninhabit-
able, there is very little available soil remain-
ing upon this globe, and Avhat remains is
being occupied with portentous rapidity.
The Australian nation is absolutely the last
great nation which can be constructed any-
where, for the Australian people hold almost
the last patch of virgin land on earth, and
Avhen this continent is filled there Avill be
little left.
# # #
And the census which has just been taken
shows that Australia is filling at least as
rapidly as could be expected, yet strangely
enough the nation seems to be pleased that
the catastrophe is coming steadily nearer.
The Old World is already suffering untold
miseries through the abundance of popula-
tion, Avith its concomitants of misery, squalor
and hunger ; and the inhabitants of this
country, with an unspeakable fatuousness,
are glad that these same evidences of civilisa-
tion are rapidly approaching in their own
land. The rival provinces of Victoria and
New South Wales have been running a race
for many years past to secure the first place
on the census-lists, and there is a unanimous
av hoop of rejoicing because New South Wales
is about 12,000 inhabitants ahead—in other
Avords, because it is 12,000 stages the nearer
to Gehenna. There is no reason ascribed for
this outburst of popular exultation ; it is
merely a matter of universal habit. The
people of England rejoice in like manner over
the increase in theircensusreturns, though the
great question of the day in England is how
or where to shunt some three million super-
fluous paupers ; the paupers remain un-
shunted because there is no place to
put them, and the country rejoices be-
cause it is growing more paupers, though
OAvning no spot to dump them down on.
The inhabitants c3f the United States are
mortified beyond measure because they only
number sixty-two millions, instead of sixty-
five as anticipated ; they have counted
niggers, Indians, and imported Sicilian mur-
derers, and regret that, even with all these
undesirable elements, the total is no larger.
Nobody imagines even for a moment that
the increase of numbers brings Avith it an in-
crease of material comfort, or that ten
million men are necessarily happier or more
intelligent or more civilised than tAvo million.
Almost everybody recognises that the over-
crowding of humanity contributes nothing
but disease, pauperism, low wages, misery,
and national degeneracy—that it tends in-
cessantly to the division of mankind into
capitalists and slaves—and that the constant
demand of all men is for some new and com-
paratively empty land where escape
from the evils which accompany their own
ceaseless multiplication. And yet the people
who own such a land rejoice because the last
chance or hope which the earth will ever see
is slipping out of their hands ; and the
people who are already oppressed by the
Aveight of numbers in older countries rejoice
because their oppression is still increasing ;
and they are both glad, because they suffer
from a vague idea that there is some un-
specified blessing in mere multitude, though
what that blessing may be no philosophy will
ever determine.
<5 # *
The only individual who profits by the in-
crease of population is the capitalist; to the
wage-earner that increase, under present
conditions, is an unmitigated curse. As the
census-returns expand rents go up, because
though land is a fixed quality humanity is
practically illimitable ; and wages go down
with the increasing intensity of competition.
The national wealth, it is true, is greater in
thickly-peopled countries than in lands where
inhabitants are scarce, and with the multipli-
cation of mankind this wealth will keep on
multiplying, but the great mass of the
nations are only so much the poorer for this
affluence. The aggregate wealth of England
is some ten thousand millions, but the bulk
of this is mademp of the alleged value of the
English soil, and the soil has an artificial
value because the Englishman must stand
somewhere, and to do that he must pay the
land-monopolist whatever price he demands
for the use of a patch of his own country. If
it were possible to monopolise the whole
water-supply of the country in the same way,
water might suddenly rise to such a market-
value that England would be ■worth twenty-
thousand millions instead of ten, but the
nation would be no richer for this
sudden accession of wealth. The one
possible excuse for the strange hunger after
population with which the civilised world is
afflicted is that increase of population means
a rapid increase of national wealth, but this
sort of wealth is poverty—not affluence.
Scarcity of land is not riches any more than
is scarcity of food. The fact that a foot of
land in Melbourne may be worth £3OO
through the steady growth of population is
of no more benefit to the nation at large
than if a loaf of bread were worth the same
amount. The country is proud of such an
evidence of prosperity, but a rat or an old,
tough hen has often fetched more per foot
frontage in the markets of a beleaguered city
than w r as ever realised for the best land in
Melbourne or Sydney, and in each case the
cause was the same—famine. In the latter
days of the famous siege of Leyden, one
dead horse would have commanded such a
price that it alone would have made a
reasonable measure of national wealth. The
increase of population certainly means
prosperity—the sort of prosperity which is
synonymous with hunger ; but apart from
this it has no special value whatever.
* * #
Yet the howl for population is loud and
vociferous throughout all this crowded
world. Such a paragraph as the following—
from the South Australian papers—is a fair
sample of how far this mania can be carried :
General Downes was interviewed to-day respecting the
work of the Military Defence Commission. He said Port
Darwin was a big place to fortify ; it had very great
possibilities, but must have Asiatic labour to make it
what it ought to be. It seemed manifest to him that
little could he done with this very fine country without
Asiatic labour. European labour was decreasing, and
most of the business was done by Chinese.
There is a fine vein of humorous idiocy about
that paragraph. It is frankly assumed that
the first essential in every country is that
population should be increasing, and as—in
tins instance—it is falling off, it is proposed
that the country should be filled up, as a last
resource, with niggers and Chinamen. A
hundred or two hundred years hence an
empty province such as the Northern Terri-
tory may be a greater blessing to the Aus-
tralian people than the sight of a new
Paradise. By that time, if the present rate
of increase is maintained, Europe, America
and Asia may all be crowded to the last
extremity, and Australia may be the only
land remaining in all this earth where there
is breathing-room left for a new nation.
The fact that the Territory is empty is no
present injury to the unborn Australian who
doesn’t inhabit it; and if it were filled up
with Asiatics the grievance would not be
abated in any way, for the Australian who
isn’t there now would still be absent. And
even if the Territory should remain neglected
for 20,000 years, there is still no reason Avhy
it should not be reserved for the Australian
nation which may overflow into it at the
end of that period. The craving to get rid
of the last available patch of vacant soil
is as hopelessly insane as the craving
to throw overboard the last remaining scraps
of food from a raft in mid-ocean ; and the
desire to increase the population of the earth
under all circumstances is more mad than
the desire to increase the population of a
blockaded city. Yet the desire for more and
still more inhabitants is so deep-rooted that
no one appears to notice the amazing dis-
crepancy. There is no more reason or
advantage in crowding up the earth than in
crowding up Gehenna, but somehow, from
sheer force of habit, it is held to be a matter
for public congratulation that the earth is
getting to be crowded.
# # *
The world cannot by any process at present
available, avert the increase of population,
but none the less its tendency to whoop and
let off rockets because of the spread of that
incurable complaint called Man is a sight to
make angels weep. ‘ As the w r aste places fill
up, the picturesque forests are cut down to
make room for gasometers and bone-boiling
establishments and civilisation ; and the
hills are blasted away to provide building
materials ; and the rivers are pumped dry to
supply irrigation works ; and by and bye
this globe promises to be one great, wide,
sad monotony of men and cities and cows,
with very little else. All this is done
that the world may grow two human beings
where only one grew before, and yet, in most
cases, it would have been better for that
other human being had he never been born
at all. Life, as a rule, has not much to offer
him. Sometimes he is a pauper, hanging
out in a city slum. Sometimes he is an
“ unemployedoccasionally he is hanged;
almost always he toils his whole life through
for a wretched pittance, amid an arid waste
of bricks and mortar and poverty and all
manner of wretchedness. And with every
million that is added to the census returns
the pittance grows more precarious, and the
waste of bricks and mortar more arid, and
the prospect of being hanged more promising,
Whether he goes to Gehenna or not at the
close of his career is a question which no
system of theology seems able to settle, so it
may be included in the reckoning or left
out, according to fancy. But even without
it, the increase of population seems a very
inferior subject for any nation to rejoice
about,
PLAIN ENGLISH.
If the Thing's had been that Weren’t.
One of the gaudy dodges for which the Austra-
lian Tory is celebrated is just now being put
into practice in South Australia. A few weeks
ago two new members were elected for the
Upper House in that province, and to the un-
speakable horror of the fossilised Chamber the
successful candidates were both returned in the
Labour interest. This was without exception
the roughest thing that had ever happened since
the Upper House first started. The spectacle
of two pronounced democrats in that stronghold
of old, coarse, Tory bigotry was worse than
that of two large hogs in a synagogue, or two
hangmen at a Primrose League banquet; and
even two dead Chinamen in Government House
would have been a good deal less objectionable.
The thing was done, however, and unless some
brilliant piece of hard swindling can be devised
it is irreparable, but all the combined Toryism
of South Australia took a great oath that such
a thing would never, never, never, by any
chance or possibility, happen again. And if
hard, unscrupulous faking of the electoral rolls
will serve the purpose it never will happen.
The Upper House at Adelaide is an elective
body, and everybody who pays £25 a year in
rent is entitled to a vote. But many thousand
electors pay rent at the rate of 10s. a week or
£26 a year, and these are being struck off the
rolls en viasse on the ground that, in most cases,
the landlord pays taxes, and if, instead, the
tenant paid the taxes and deducted them
from the rent, he would then, most
probably, pay less than £25. This unclean
fraud was got up by the Government, which
has issued printed directions to the returning-
officers on the subject. As it happens, the
Act gives no special directions on the subject,
which makes this great political swindle all the
more glaring. It is merely stipulated that the
voter must pay £25 a year dear, but there is
absolutely no connection between rent and
taxes that any sane mind can discover. Of course
no one can deny that if a tenant paid his land-
lord’s rates, and deducted them from his
present rent of £26 a year, he might then pay
less than £25, but the brief and comprehensive
answer of the Labour party is that he does
nothing of the kiijd, and what he might do if he
did something else has no bearing on the case.
He might'pay his landlord’s meat-bill, for that
matter, and deduct it from the rent, and then,
if the landlord were a heavy feeder, he might
not have a qualification, even though his rent
were really £5O per annum. He might pay for
his landlord’s funeral, too, or he might have
three legs and a head made out of lard, or he
might never have been born at all, in which
case he wouldn’t have had a vote under any
circumstances. When a Government goes in
for knocking its opponents off the roll because
they would have been disqualified if something
had occurred that didn’t take place, it opens
up a very large question, for the things that
haven’t happened are almost as numerous as
the people who weren’t born. There is a deep
and boundless ingenuity about a system of this
kind, but it only applies to one of the two great
parties that make up the State. Who is there,
for instance, who could paint the frozen glare
that would shine in the eye of Property if any
working-man applied to have a dozen big syndi-
cates struck off the roll on the ground that,
under certain possible circumstances which
might have happened but didn’t, they also
might not have had a qualification, but might
have been in gaol for vagrancy instead ?

Trades Hall and Government House.
A eew weeks ago, when certain of the Mel-
bourne Trades Hall people questioned — not on
social, but financial grounds, alas ! —the ad-
visability of spending the thick end of £IOU on
champagne and grovel for Lord Hopetoun, a
very superficial democrat objected that nobody
from the Hall received invitations to Govern-
ment Bouse. It was an ignominious complaint
to make, because- the omission to bestow
“cards” upon labour representatives might
well have been accepted as a compliment to
their democracy—a dumb confession that there
is no place in a vice-regal function for any man
whose business it is to consolidate the forces
which sassiety— viz. the worshippers of caste —
would fain scatter. Few, probably, among the
bitterest opponents of the Trades Hall imagined
that its leaders coveted the entree to Govern-
ment House, or indulged in day-dreams of rub-
bing shoulders with such as hold aides-de-camp
sacred. The notion was too absurd. Every-
body is supposed to know by this time that his
Excellency is not so much an institution foi'
rewarding personal merit with a hand-shake;
as merely the “ Home ’’-appointed head of a
local snobocracy. To treat as a privilege that
which a fourth-rate globe-trotter regards as a
right is to display a singular ignorance of hoW
the world wags, an ignorance unpardonable to
the controlleis of a powerful organisation.
Tie Trades Hall should engage a needy
“Johnnie” in the capacity of social adviseb


and get him to report upon Government House,
and the qualifications for admission to its outer
circle. An older and “ wider ” gentleman than
Hobetoun would perhaps have paid no heed
to the growl of the toiler who wanted to see,
at least, one of his chiefs figuring in the list of
guests at a vice-regal shivoo. He might not
have cared to risk a refusal of his cheap invita-
tion under galling conditions. Hobetoun, how-
ever, jumped to the conclusion that Labour was
smarting under a sense of neglect, and itching
to send a delegate in dress-clothes to stand
around in the marble halls dedicated to the
Queen’s representative. So Secretary Wal-
lington was told to try the effect of a ticket
upon Mr. J. T. Sitch, President of the Trades
Hall Council, and that guileless magnate was
‘ ‘ nobbled ” as easily as the first favourite in a
selling-race. In fact, Hobetoun may be said
to have nobbled the whole Council. At their
next meeting Sitch arose, with emotion in his
aspect, and informed his colleagues that he had
been invited to a party at Government House,
which he would make a point of attending.
Whereat all present expressed their satisfaction
by cheers. They positively gloated over the
thought that one out of some eight hundred
invitations had been sent to a working-man.
It would be vastly interesting to The Bulletin
to learn whether the Trades-Unionists, as a
body, feel complimented .by Lord Hobetoun’s
patronage on this occasion. It is not necessary
to maintain that an invitation to plain Sitch,
as a private person, wouldn’t mean much, be-
cause hundreds of sitch (overlook it) invitations
are slung off at a time, and a “Government
House young man,” who has neither brains,
“position,” nor even cash, but merely a Scotch
letter of introduction to recommend him, may
be found in any genteel boarding-house. The
question of how much value may be attached to
a ticket for Sitch, the President, is worth con-
sidering. In the first place, it only came as an
answer to the protest of a misguided individual
who didn’t know what he was talking about.
In the next place, if it were intended as a recog-
nition of the dignity of manual labour, it evi-
dently estimates that dignity at J per cent,
as against 99J per cent, of dignity represented
by the uppah, or non-labouring, class. Mr.
Sitch could do no good to his cause by playing
the part of a needle in a bundle of hay. A
Governor is an apology for Victoria Regina,
and his house is an apology for her Court. The
atmosphere of the one place may be less ener-
vating and demoralising than that of the other,
but it smells considerably of Court, neverthe-
less. And should Sitch go there often he will
become tainted with the prevailing odour, and
his Trades Hall constituents will find that the
only way of fitting a square peg to a round hole
is by trimming the peg.

“ Truckling - to Labour.”
The British press has solemnly resolved at last
that the failure of the recent Victorian and
South Australian loans arises from one well-
defined cause—the “Truckling to Labour ” by
the various Australian Governments, which has
awakened alarm as to the safety of Property,
and that dull, apopletic, fat-headed class which
is known as “all right-thinking persons,” has
consequently held aloof. Also, the thick, bald,
stertorous-breathing individual, commonly de-
scribed as a “man with a well-balanced mind,”
has recognised the danger of the situation, and
the parochial being, who is defined as ‘‘ an in-
telligent and respected citizen,” has done the
same thing. It was a London Tory newspaper
which first noticed this alarming tendency to
“ truckle to Labour,” but since it made the dis-
covery all the Tory press of Great Britain has
awakened to a knowledge of the approaching peril,
and they have unanimously agreed that the Aus-
tralian Governments are truckling to Labour in
a most alarming manner. Also they recommend
the statesmen of Australia to abandon this
nefarious practice at once. Curiously enough
every one of these Tory snufiiers seems to take
it for granted that Labour and the Government
are two entirely distinct affairs, and not one of
them seems to realise that as the labouring-
classes form the majority of the nation, and the
nation is supposed to be governed by the
majority, therefore the Labour party, according
to the British constitution, ought to be the
Government. Even in England, if the working
classes were united, they could swamp the
House of Lords, the Established Church, the
Tory party, and the famous British Crown in
one act, but, strangely enough, the modern Tory
has never found out this fact. And when he
has failed to see the change that is going on
right under his own port-wine-flushed nose, it
is not surprising that he can’t understand how
things are situated in Australia. When the
Queensland strike broke out it was evidently
expected that the hereditary Australian nobility
would have called out its men-at-arms and
retainers, and fortified the old feudal castles
and loaded up the culverin, or the demi-
john, or something of that sort, and cast
the strikers into the lowest dungeon beneath
the castle-moat. It was anticipated, probably,
that the Trades Hall Council would have been
sent in chains to Thursday Island, and the
Finance Committee tracked by bloodhounds,
and that the sporting community would have
gone out shooting miscellaneous democrats, and
bagged a good many disaffected citizens of all
sorts and descriptions. The Tory has never
yet quite realised that this sort of government
is out of date even in England. He loves to
cavort round in some semblance of the old
feudal style, and to go forth—in his mind’s
eye—and gallop over the “ lower orders and
his famous organ, the Saturday Review,
suggested recently that perhaps, after all, per-
sonal slavery was a necessary condition of
civilisation ; and little more than 30 years ago—
at Die close of the Mutiny—an eminent Tory
official in India suggested that the system of
judicial torture should be revived in order to
extract “ confessions ” from the Sepoy
prisoners, and the suggestion was formally
considered, and only rejected “as a matter of
policy.” A party of this sort naturally feels
hurt when it sees any Government truckling to
Labour. The Government, it is true, granted
not one solitary concession to Labour, but it
manifested an ill-advised hesitation, whereas it
ought to have had the standing army tearing
through the streets, and banging the brains out
of everybody who was found out of doors. And
when it failed to do this, all right-thinking-
persons objected to lend any more money, for
there is a fixed impression in the British intelli-
gence that a Government which isn’t covered
with blood, and which doesn’t look like a
butcher dancing in a slaughter-house, can’t be
trusted with cash any longer. If Australia
wants to borrow money it must stop ‘ ‘ truckling
to Labour ” for the future, and keep right on
truckling to Property instead, that being
the only thing any respectable State ever
truckles to.

A “Freetrade Tariff.”
When the Parkes Government put up Mr.
M‘Millan, a few days ago, to maunder about
the coming Australian Federation and various
other matters, it revealed a vast expanse of hog-
like ignorance with which it had not generally
been credited. Most of the public utterances
of the Parkes Government have been somewhat
obscure of late, owing to bricks and dead
animals, but still it always managed to say
enough to make a fool of itself in a large and
liberal sense. The Parkes crowd, as The
Bulletin foretold weeks ago, are running on
the combined issues of Foreign-trade and Federa-
tion, but they are doing it even more clumsily
than was expected. The single obvious fact
about Federation is that it must bring all-round
Protection in its train, for when one alleged
Freetrade province federates with five Protec-
tionist provinces, the result is as much a fore-
gone conclusion as the fate of one dog that is
stepped on by five elephants. Moreover, when
one-half the dog is in favour of the stepping-on
process the last possibility of doubt is removed,
and the Foreign-trade creed is gone beyond all
recovery. So far, Parkes has judiciously
dodged all reference to this dilemma, but
M‘Millan, who rushes in where angels and
other animals would gladly avoid going, pro-
fesses to take an entirely different view of the
situation. He claims, in the first place, that
the other provinces will quite probably abandon
their Protectionist policy and accept Freetrade
—that the party which is fast going to inevit-
able smash in New South Wales will run the
entire show—and, in fact, that half a province
is a majority, and five provinces and a-half are
a minority. A man who can believe a thing
like that can believe anything, and in that
great day when the hog takes unto itself
wings he will assuredly have his reward.
And, in addition to this, he asserts that ‘‘ a
Freetrade tariff” is the one bestsuited for United
Australia, from which it is evident that the
small, unconsidered M‘Millan is a hundred
and twenty times more foolish than anybody
ever supposed. For the very essence of Free-
trade is that trade shall be free and that there
shall be no tariff at all, and a Freetrade tariff is
as hopelessly anomalous as a white nigger, or a
live corpse that stands up horizontally on its
back and speaks in a speechless utterance with
its feet. Consequently, if there is Freetrade, the
Federal Government will have nothing to live
upon, for it is already agreed that the Federal
administration is to be kept up out of the
Customs, revenue, and on a Freetrade basis
there won’t be any Customs. In the face of
such statements as these it is not surprising
that the members of the Parkes Government
have been greeted of late by their constituents
with flour-bags and so on; and as for the
assertion made by one Foreign-trade paper
that the missiles were heaved by Protectionists,
and by another that they were shied by
Socialists, one of these declarations must be
hard lying, and probably both were equally
mendacious. In its present state of disorgani-
sation, in fact, it would hardly be surprising if
the Parkes Cabinet was out throwing road-
metal at itself, and committing assault and
battery on itself in public places. A Freetrade
aggregation which is helping to organise a 'Pro-
tectionist League with a view to abolishing
Customs duties is so badly mixed that it is
capable of any enormity under the sun, and
when it deliberately holds up its awful mess to
view on the end of a pitchfork and invites the
public to look on it with admiration, the worst
results may be expected. Any ordinary sample
of mud would get up and throw itself under
such conditions. No wonder Parkes asks
people to look only at Federation, and not at
the consequences of Federation. '
An Expression of Democracy.
As Sir George Grey observed, no more solemn
and wonderful sight ever met the eye of man
than that of the vast assemblage drawn together
in the immense Sydney Centennial Hall on May
26 to hear his lecture on “ The Vitals of Demo-
cracy.” It was no ordinary crowd that
assembled, but a seething ocean of humanity
which surged up against the bulwarks of the
platform, foamed up into the galleries, and un-
availingly dashed itself against the choked
entrances. Everywhere billows of human
beings, crowned with a white surf of faces, ever
turned towards the platform—billows whose
regularity of line was broken by the hun-
dreds who filled the aisles and stood
against the walls. In the central aisle
there were probably a thousand afoot, so
densely packed as to be deprived of proper
foothold, swaying all the while from side to
side, like corn that is stirred by light airs.
Outside in the corridors over a thousand men
struggled vainly for ingress, and the rush of
their hurried feet, their hoarse clamour sounded
within like the dashing of waves into an ocean
cave. To no man who ever set foot in Austra-
lasia, to few men throughout the world, has
such an obviously genuine ovation been accorded
as that which fell to the lot of Sir George
Grey, the other evening. When he entered
the hall the cheers were deafening, but when he
stood up to speak the people seemed transported
with enthusiasm : the rumbling of all the
artillery of heaven, the hoarse roar of
an angry sea on a rockbound coast,
the sound of a hundred organs with all the
stops out, would not have the volume and
resonance of that mighty outburst that went
up from at least six thousand throats. As a
lecture—the reference is not to its matter —The
Bulletin does not scruple to say that the
affair was a failure, for the veteran’s feeble
pipe vainly struggled to fill the vast building,
But there were no interruptions, and although
one-third of the audience could not hear the
address, few left their places during the two
hours occupied in its delivery. The people of
Sydney had assembled not so much to hear a
speech as to pay tribute to an illustrious leader
of the commons, and the result was one of the
most magnificent expressions of belief in demo-
cratic institutions that the world has ever
witnessed within walls. Were Grey— the
people’s tribune—thirty years younger, there
is no radical reform that he could not success-
fully captain. He would lead the people of
Australasia to better things, as Moses did the
Israelites through the desert to the Promised
Land. But his rate of progression would be
swifter than that of the Hebrew law-giver.
Another Clean Bowl-out.
Dear Bulletin, —Recently a trumped-up sensa-
tion about a unionist shearer poisoning a soldier
at Barcaldine, Q., fizzled ingloriously out;
although, by the way, the newspapers which
displayed the alleged poisoning in large head-
ings forgot to mention the Queensland Govern-
ment chemist’s disproving analysis of the
“ poisoned ” man’s vomit. That horror having
bubble-bursted, another has been manufactured
—I use the word advisedly—still more sensa-
tional. I quote the following telegram from
the Sydney Daily Telegraph :
POISONING STATION WATER-TANKS.
Brisbane, Thursday.— A number of unionists have
lately been seen on the Mitchell Downs country, and as
certain actions of theirs in connection with one of the
tanks from which the supply of drinking water was drawn
by the boundary riders excited suspicion it was decided
to submit a sample of the water to an analytic examina-
tion. Sergeant Bcrke filled a bottle from the tank and
immediately sealed it. This was forwarded to the Colonial
Secretary’s office and submitted for analysis to Mr. Mar,
Government analyst. His report, which has been for-
warded to the Colonial Secretary, states that he found
the water to contain a solution of as much strychnine as
the water was capable of dissolving, or more than one
grain per pint. He further reports that undissolved
strychnine was found as sediment in the bottle which
contained the water.
The Sydney Morning Herald also published a
similar statement, without deliberately connect-
ing unionists with the poisoning. The ‘ ‘ in-
ventor,” whoever he may be, of this strychnine
scare has overdone his part. He evidently
didn’t know the characteristics of strychnine in
relation to its solubility. Now, strychnine is
soluble in distilled water only 1 in 5760, or 1§
grains per imperial pint; in this tank, we are
told, the water was ‘ ‘ capable of dissolving more
than one grain per pint”—which shows that
the water was fairly pure. But Government
Analyst Mar “further reports that undissolved
strychnine was found as sediment in the bottle
which contained the water." This gives the
whole thing away. Who imposed upon the
police-sergeant by putting that “ undissolved
strychnine ” into the bottle I If the tank-water
had dissolved all the strychnine which it could
take up, the alkaloid in solution would not be
precipitated as sediment —and pure strychnine
at that! —by mere transfer to a bottle. Any
chemist will tell you that. It is clear that
strychnine was conveyed into the sealed bottle,
forwarded by the police-sergeant. Who so
conveyed it ? Deduction : —Either the tank-
water was uupoisoued and only the test-bottle-
ful was poisoned; or else the tank-water was
poisoned and the test-bottleful over-poisoned.
It is lucky the practical joker didn’t know
enough chemistry to enable him to make strych-
nine form soluble salts. If the dimensions of
the tank were known, it would be interesting
to work out the problem as to how much
strychnine would have to be thrown into the
water to enable that fluid to take up all the
strychnine it is capable of dissolving.—Yours,
&c., Chemist.

Parkes, 21st May, 1891.

Parkes, 28th May, 1891.
He (Dibbs) told the House
that, if the Government de-
layed submitting the resolu-
tion on the Draft Bill of the
Convention, some threecolo-
nies would come together
and form a Federation fx-om
which this colony would be
excluded. Now, that seemed
to him to be akin to the low
motives of neax-Jy the whole

or any man of intelligence

colonies were ready to rush
and take some mean advan-
tage of New South Wales
on that question of Federa-

hold water for a moment.
Sir Henry Parkes’ present definition of the
status of the Convention’s Constitution Bill is
that no Australian Parliament can “ touch a
letter or comma ” of it; but that each Parlia-
ment can suggest amendments, excisions, and
recastings, and that these must be submitted
to “a Convention of the same character, and
same power, and, in like manner, representing
all the colonies, as the Convention which
framed” the present Bill. Copeland had no
hesitation in describing Parkes’ manoeuvring
as a “trap” and a “ trick,” and argued that if
a second Conference “refused to agree with”
the suggested amendments of the various Parlia-
ments, “ the Bill as now constituted would
have to be submitted to the country, and must
become the Constitution of the Federal States.”
There are chinks in Copeland’s logic, since
“the country” might reject the twice-offered
Bill, or again refer it with suggestions of
amendment; all the same, he is not far out
when he describes the Premier’s finessing as a
‘ 1 trap. ” It is an artful trap ; more especially
is this seen when the coercive nature of the
clause enabling three provinces to federate in
advance of the rest is considered.
W hen Colonel De Wolski, the energetic and
vigorous Imperial officer just now in the N.S.
Wales Service (and who is hugely disgusted
at the incapacity and ignorance of his depart-
mental chiefs), found out that the Bare Island
forts were formed on rubbish instead of upon
the concrete which had been paid for with tax-
payers’ money, one of the persons bowled out
became very nasty and threatening, whereupon
the Colonel promised to throw him into the sea.
It is quite a pity that Colonel Dk Wolski has
not legal power to throw, into the sea all con-
cerned in one of the most disgraceful and
treasonable jobs ever literally unearthed. It
would appear that the contractors, M‘Leod and
Co., “tipped in” loads of “rubble-stone,
pipeclay, and dirt,” without a trace of cement,
as a substitute for concrete, and as a conscien-
tious reward for their “extra” services they
charged the Government £33,857 4s. 10d.,
instead of the original amount of their tender,
£25,020 15s. 4d. The then Colonial Archi-
tect, Mr. Barnet, accepted their tender for
the erection of adjoining barracks, and did not
report to the Minister for Works for eight
months the fact of acceptance. Clerks of Works
Colley and Purkis departmentally supervised
the works, after the manner of their kind—•
Colley visiting Bare Island quite three times a
year, and Purkis, the permanent supervisor,
two or three times a week, while both Colley
and Purkis had “every confidence in” the
contractors. Similarly, M‘Leod left everything
to his manager and sometime partner, Noble.
Then Purkis was good enough to borrow £750
from M ‘Leod. The careless Barnet —for nobody
thinks lie was more than careless having
retired on a full pension, is to be let severely
alone. Colley and one M ‘Shane are to be
pensioned off. Purkis is to show cause why he
should not be dismissed. M‘Leod is to be
asked to refund £6343 3s. 5d., and more when
other calculations are finished, and, if neces-
sary, sued for the amounts ; and both he and
Noble are to be debarred from tendering
again for Government works. The stiff-necked,
inartistic, and unpoetical De W olski is to be
accorded a vote of thanks for spoiling every-
body else’s little horn spoon,

Compare the New South Wales election fore-
casts of a few prominent politicians; multiply
them by the number of candidates; divide by
the number of seats; apply the “rule of three ”
to the number of issues, the number of parties,
and the number of electorates ; shake the result
in your hat, and discern the solution . Dikes
declares that the elections will give him “a
large majority ; ” Barton says they will evolve
an anti-Federation party, and yet show that ‘ ‘ a
large majority of the people ” are favour-
able to Federation in the abstract, though
opposed to the Convention’s Bill ; Reid
thinks they will be “eminently beneficial to
the Freetrade party ” ; Garvan, that they will
“ bring back a decided preponderance of mem-
bers antagonistic to Freetrade,” and a much
larger number of labour representatives, but
that there will not be “ any large party formed
distinctly on the lines of its opposition to
Federation ” ; and Want holds that a strong
anti-federation party will certainly be formed,
and that a “ much stronger Freetrade party will
be returned.” What is the value of any one or
all of these forecasts ? Alsolutely nil.

Mr. Supreme Court Judge Chubb (N.Q.), in
Regina v. Yosper, for “sedition”:—
There is no moral harm in advocating a Republic, pro-
vided peaceful means are recommended. I believe, and 1
think in common with most intelligent men, that the
conversion of the Australian colonies into a Republic
is inevitable, and I am of opinion that the person who
does not, in some measure, agree with that idea is one
who is incapable of comprehending the destiny of his
country. . . Success alone justifies rebellion ;ifit is
successful, then, whether the effects are good or ill, no one
can judge of them, and none can punish the principals;
if, however, it is not successful, society avenges itself on
the rebels, even though it may subsequently admit that
they were right. ... If the Crown placed the same
construction upon Chief Justice Lilley’s words that they
had upon those of the defendant, and the jury endorsed
that construction, then the Chief Justice could be
punished as could be any other individual.
Now, then, Premier Griffith. Are you
going to have Mr. Justice Chubb arraigned
before both Houses of Parliament? And do
you expect to secure an absolute majority of
both Chambers to deprive him of his seat on
the Supreme Court Bench ? Hey !

Ex-Speaker Groom, of Queensland, advocates
a tax on wool. He draws attention to this
point;—
Look at the country through which the railway runs
out to the West. Nothing but sheep on it. You may
look in vain for homesteads after leaving Cowrie Junc-
tion. The railways run through magnificent country, but
it contains no human beings.
True, every word of it. Yet, these landholders
are the pirates who have stolen the land from
the people, and the canoodlers who legislated to
secure to their own class a 21 years’ lease of all
the other available Queensland country, who
are now breathing mock moral sentiments about
“law and order,” and have persuaded the
Government, also of their own kidney, to back
them up in the wholesale importation of black-
legs by means of gatling-guns and soldiers at an
expenditure of £BOOO a week. Small wonder
that unionist shearers, feeling the gall of the
yoke, sometimes curse, and even yell out
“Whoa’’and “Boo,” upon finding the whole
unbearable weight of class government pressing-
on their burdened backs.
The suppression of Postmaster-General O’Con-
nor is fast becoming a crying necessity in New
South Wales. Foreign visitors suffer from an
impression that Daniel O’Connor is New
South Wales, and that all New South Wales is
Daniel O’Connor, and this idea brings the pro-
vince into contempt. It is unnecessary to men-
tion that Daniel delivered an address to Sara
Bernhardt when that eminent Frenchwoman
arrived last week ; he would have done that if
he had been dead, and he reads an address -to
everybody. He delivers speeches of welcome
to footballers, dukes, scullers, prominent
aboriginals, Home Rulers, poets, literary men,
troops, consuls, revivalists, and new Governors
—in fact, nobody of any note ever arrives in
Sydney without the Postmaster-General seizing
that person and bending him violently forward
over his stomach in an affectionate embrace.
He goes out and wobbles violently on the
heaving deep when anybody is expected off the
port, and greets him in the name of the nation.
When anybody is buried he discharges an un-
grammatical oration over the grave, and in-
cidentally conveys an impression that he and


the deceased were boys together, quite regard
less of the fact that the deceased may be 30
years younger or 40 years older than himself.
He proposes everybody’s health, and is
the last man to weep over everybody
when that individual quits the country
amid the regrets of the entire populace.
When anybody does anything he is on hand to
embrace him, and hint that he knew all along
that he was going to do it; and when anybody
fails to do anything, he is the first man to clear
out of his company with a reproachful look in
his eye, which says plainly that he knew he
wasn’t going to do it. He is present at every
banquet, where he looks as if he knew more
than the Encyclopedia Bextannica, and when
anybody of importance happens to die, Daniel
O’Connor is at the door inquiring about him in
his last moments. If an emperor be blown up,
the Postmaster-General looks as if he used to
sleep in the same bed with that emperor ; and
when any new place is discovered for the first
time, he wears the expression of a man who has
been there frequently. But some day or other,
in his anxiety to embrace a celebrated visitor
before anybody else gets hold of him, he will
go and embrace a smallpox patient by mistake,
and come to grief in consequence. But he is
very slow to do this, and, meanwhile, he makes
the country feel tired.

The sisters of a man—a lunatic—recently
executed in Australia, have been compelled to
leave the town in which they lived owing to
“public feeling;” while a brother, who contri-
buted to their support, has been requested to re-
sign his position in the same city, on account of
pressure put by customers upon his employers.
When a man is judicially butchered, all his people
are socially butchered with him.
S, M. Herald is supporting Parkes, and
has obligingly supplied him with a cry wherein to
appeal to the country. The Herald says :
“The great dominant question to be decided will be—
Is this colony for Australian unity and intercolonial
freedom of trade, or against it?
The Bulletin says that the great dominant
question is—ls N.S.W. for Australian unity and
Protection against the world ? The case thus
stated may not suit the Herald and Parkes, but
that is the exact case notwithstanding.
The Labour Party appears to be practically
unorganised in the one-horse Conservative island
of Tasmania, whose chief periodical thrill, pre-
vious to its late silver boom, used to be a spasm of
yearning to be annexed to Victoria, a consumma-
tion that the last-named province did not hanker
after. As Victoria is, however, practically run-
ning the silver boom, Tasmanian “annexationists”
ought to be satisfied. The Labour candidates
were nnmistakeably downed at the recent general
flections in the Island—the miners’ candidate,
Haslett, being badly defeated for Cumberland,
which includes the Zeehan silver-field ; and Mac-
donald and Dugan, laboui’-eandidates, being
sinkers in South Launceston, Dugan losing his
£25 deposit. Nevertheless, things are brightening
and the day will come.
“Old” Kearney (A. J, K.), a veteran
Australian newspaper-man, passed in his last
screed one day last week. In the olden days of
Melbourne journalism, Kearney was one of the
smartest all-round men then going. Melbourne,
Hobart, Townsville, Rockhampton, Brisbane’
Sydney, and doubtless, many Australian bush
towns, have suffered newspaper-education at his
haUds, his lines gradually but surely falling in
more and more uncongenial places. Poor
Kearney ! Old age found him with one solitary
but precious tooth in his gums, turning out
white-haired screed for Sydney Evening News
—the self same Kearney who, we remember,
wrote a capital article for the very first number of
The Bulletin. There is a good Hobart story
about Kearney. He was in that city of melan-
choly stagnation stone-broke, with an uncertain
screed-pittance coming in, and unable to raise the
money to take him out of Tasmania. One night
in a nectar-shop, a press comrade waggishly sug-
gested that Kearney might raise the wind by
giving a lecture, saying that he would get the
necessary tickets printed, “If you do,” added
Boniface, entering into the joke, “I’ll hire the
Town Hall for you.” “Gimme a subject,” said
Kearney. “Oh, on ‘Everything in general and
nothing in particular.’” “I’ll doit,” gurgled Kear-
ney, “but I’ve not got a dress-suit.” “Ican lend
you a swallow-tail coat,” said his friend. “I’ll
lend you a waistcoat,” cried another. “And I’ll
lend you black trousers,” added the landlord. The
joke became an earnest enterprise. The lecture
came off. The Town Hall was filled, and Kearney
appeared on the stage also “very full,” and
dressed in trousers that only reached half-way
down his ankles, and betrayed the fact that he
wore no socks, a baggy vest that could have easily
concealed a bolster, and a crinkled-up coat
about four sizes too small. Also Boniface’s
gold watch and chain and pendants. This
gorgeous scarecrow, speaking without notes,
commenced inflicting the most hideous babble
cm the audience. One disgusted citizen got
up in about 10 minutes and started for the door.
“ Stop, my friend,” said Kearney, The citizen
turned round. “You’re not going out?” “I
am, I’ve had enough of this rot.” “Are you
sure you’re going ?” “Yes, confound you !” and
the citizen made another break for the door.
“You’re quite sure you’re going ?” “Yes, I tell
you.” “ Then, ‘moind the shtep as yez go’”
-a theatrical gag then current, which fairly
brought down the house and broke up the lecture.
As Kearney retired ho was closely pursued by
Boniface, and the owners of the “dress ’’garments,
who feared misadventures. But he raised his
passage-money all right.

The Sara Bernhardt enterprise started
at Melbourne Princess’ with a play already un-
favourably known to 80 per cent., perhaps, of the
great audience assembled. To suppose that many
of them came to enjoy “ Camille ” —the wire-
drawn love-story of a sentimental young sensualist
and a consumptive courtesan —would be absurd.
They came charged with the expectation of seeing
5.8., and ready either to be disillusioned by the
bearer of a big name, or to feel convinced that
French plays, at advanced rates, must inevitably
prove “no go” in Australia. Sara Bernhardt
upset this conviction by a performance which
soared high above the hopes of all, save the few
who had seen her in other parts and places.
People who, not being French, were content to
rely upon their English recollections of the dif-
ferent characters in the play and the unfolding of
its plot, instead of attempting to follow the dia-
logue in a book, were fascinated by Bernhardt’s
charm of face and gesture, moved by her nervous
force, and always persuaded by her sweet natural-
ness. As a Frenchwoman, she talks with a rapidity
that bewilders the bookworms entirely, but as an
actress she speaks with voice, eyes, arms, or
feet, if necessary, the eternal cosmopolitan
language of the soul. (N.B. —This is intended
for a burst of enthusiasm.) The dullest pluto-
crat in the “ circle ” must have understood
Marguerite Gautier when, after hearing Armand’s
vigils under her window, she strikes the keynote
of the trouble in one soft, lingering sentence—
“ e’est tres gentil, cela.” The book translates this
into “ very nice ! ” but it means a whole volume
of poetry. To- particularise all the points at which
Sara asserted her divinity in its [gentler phases
would take too long. Besides, a special war cor-
respondent could convey no adequate idea of the
awful battle of her emotions, and the heartrending
moans of her wounded feelings in the scene which
is ended by Armand flinging her down and throw-
ing bits of paper at her. As for the death, there
isn’t a corpse can touch it. Bernhardt is for all
stage purposes a lovely young creature, certainly
more delightful to the naked eye than she was 11
years ago. Time has reverently filled up the
former hollows in her cheeks and rounded her
arms to the sculptor’s ideal. After it is all over
one recalls to mind the fact that she wore ravish-
ing costumes, but not while she is on the stage
does Worth (of Paris) get his due. Things (a
suitable word !) were quite otherwise in the case
of Sara’s angular ghost, Mrs. Brown-Potter.
The company form a fairly bright tail to the comet
and are evidently higher-class performers than the
luckless crowd imported here some three years
ago. If Armand and De tlrville lack style,
according to English notions, they are at least
decent actors. A quite distingue-looking little
party appeared in a minor character ; the old
Duval shaped as a heavy father should, and a
rollicking low comedian has the best artificial
bald-head ever seen in this country. It is suf-
ficiently natural to set a ballet-girl dreaming of
bouquets and diamonds—especially diamonds. The
minor ladies seem middling, and that the show
was suitably staged goes without saying. It may
be reckless to prophesy success for a season of
foreign performances at foreign prices. But we
here hazard a belief that Sara Bernhardt will fill
the Princess’ every night for the next four weeks,
Day of Rest excepted, and twice on Saturdays.
The reception given her on her first appearance,
and Sara’s reception of the reception, were things
to be remembered. Few imagined that Austra-
lians were capable of such genuine admiration for
any lady in p, long dress. Unfortunately, they
spoilt the effect of an attempt to sing the “ Mar-
seillaise ” (without the -words) by roaring “Rule
Britannia ” directly afterwards.
* * *
Williamson and Garner wanted no speech
from the rostrum before the seats for the Bern-
hardt premiere were auctioned, but the presiding
Baillieu was not to be denied. The aristocracy of
Melbourne were before him, and he eloquently
explained that their duty to society and the
drama required them to state, in plain figures,
whether the stars of Europe were to shoot across
to this hemisphere, or hover always round the
haunts of an older civilisation. That fetched
them to the tune of 255. average for circle, and
17s. fid. for reserved stall seats. “ Cleopatra ”
will probably be brought under the hammer in
the same way.
* ** *
“LaTosca” on Monday strengthened the
first night opinion that Bernhardt has come here
to conquer. In Sardou’s play no trace of the
artist’s former ideality was perceivable, and she
was somewhat more buxom-looking in her Italian
dress of the period. Sara started off as a joyous,
wheedling young thing, whose jealousy finds vent
in feminine tantrums. It is the jealousy of a
warm-blooded girl, not of a Family Herald
heroine. After her lover had fallen into Scarpia’s
clutches, Tosca remained petulant until she be-
came aware that the assistant tormentor was
giving her poor boy fits in the adjoining room,
whereupon her aspect changed as suddenly as her
nature and she gave a representation of despair
that was utterly appalling. This, end of the
third act, moved the house even more than
Tosca’s sticking and laying out of the wicked
baron—perhaps because the public blood was
by that time frozen. The brief, final scene froze
it harder still. At her most tragic moments
Bernhardt does nothing that seems traditional or
studied. In her dealings with the corpse she
strikes no attitude which betrays her consciousness
of the folks in front. French enthusiasts who
say Sara’s histrionic genius has come from the
clouds will not be contradicted in Melbourne.
The Armand of Saturday made an acceptable
lover _ again on this occasion. The hungry
socialist who dies in a well was unpicturesquely
played by a stoutish party, but the actor who
made a first appearance as Scarpia was a decided
success on lines quite different to Bellew’s.
Several minor characters were also played most
conscientiously, and if the mounting fall's short of
the Potter production in point of splendour, it is
sometimes more suitable. The season threatens,
however, to be haunted by a seedy carpet which
went right through “ Camille,” and still had pos-
session of the floor on Monday. This acre of
faded Axminster would look fresher for a sound
thumping in the back-yard. They play “La
Tosca ” on Friday and Saturday nights, and
“Cleopatra” will probably fall a victim to the
asp on Monday,
** * &
When the large floral devices and high-
toned emblems were being shovelled up from the
orchestra, after the first act of “La Dame aux
Camelias,” a small wreath —the kind of thing that
a child hangs on a tombstone—was thrown to
Bernhardt from the amphitheatre. She picked up
that tribute, leaving the expensive vegetables to be
looked after by the funny man. It was quite a
touching incident, and let us hope that it hadn’t
been readied up beforehand.
** * *
“ The Idler” drew large houses during his
first week at Melbourne Bijou, and seems likely
to suit the spirit of this Bernhardtian era. Cart-
wright’s uncanny conception of the mother-fond
bairn is not generally admired, but the other two
gentlemen from Golden Valley decidedly changed
for the better after the first night. Titheradge put
a coat of chalk over his ruddy glow and no longer
gives one the impression of a baronet who makes
hay whilst the sun shines, but looks like the rising
parliamentarian, pallied o’er by the recollection of
having been a wild dawg, an undisciplined dingo,
in days gone by. Also Boucicault lost no time
in discarding his lounging-jacket for a frock-coat
of formal cut, which is more suggestive of Wall-
street and the almighty dollar. Still, it is
hard to see how he can float Yankee com-
panies successfully unless he buys a huge
moustache. There is something very young and
amateurish about this Simeon Strong. The
derision with which he receives Titheradge’s
explanation of the shooting incident is worthy of
a schoolboy. “You didn’t chuck a humming-top
at my little brother ! Ho, no-o ! Of course not.
You meant to hit the pear-tree, you did'—didn’t
he, Mark ?” Miss Nethersole’s appeal to Simeon
is another instance of short-weight in the author’s
text, and even that charming young manneriste
(new word), with all her intensity, is unable to
make more of it. However, “ The Idler ” has a
real strong backbone of human interest, besides
being naughty in the main idea and nice in several
small particulars.
»* * *
“Bridget O’Brien ” continues to enjoy a
high Hibernian time of it at the Royal, and it is
evident that Sheridan’s patent for an exception-
able female impersonation will hold good through
a series of comedies. By and bye the Widdy can
marry one of her pursuers and bring down the
final curtain to a roar of sympathy by throwing
her baffled spouse but of window, or something. For
it seems that the fair proprietress of Alphonso-
Napoleon is a demon when roused, like the
British lion. In point of fact, Sheridan has been
having a Little Peach duet with somebody
belonging to an opposition show and it
is averred, whether truly or not this de-
ponent cannot say, that he assaulted the
other person with a thick stick. Which
reminds us that the little man has talked of blood-
shed on previous occasions. American artists—
clever or otherwise—are very apt to hanker after
gore, and some of them are herewith enjoined to
keep their hands in their pockets and their tongues
between their teeth. Peace on earth is better than
arnica on your bruises, and one man is as good as
another when they both mean business, appar-
ently.
** * *
At the Alexandra they are doing solid
business with “ The Scout.” Lily Dampier now
shoots the neck off a, bottle with as much calm
precision as distinguishes the descent of Carver’s
horse into the tank, whilst the Injuns are becom-
ing patterns for the old style of pale-face super,
who can’t be made to look furious anyway.
** * *
To-night* (Thursday) they start “Poor
Jonathan” at the Opera House. The Clara
Merivale Company includes Snazelle, Woodfield,
and Forde, of the old brigade, with Leumane and
Ida Osborne taken over from the “ Firm.”
** * *
At Melbourne Town Hall (where Ernest
Hutcheson satisfied his friends on Saturday), the
Hallbs were to re-appear on Tuesday night. Ten
thousand voices are to chortle the new Australian
Anthem at the Exhibition next Saturday, the
halls are offering their good old “ host of attrac-
tions,” and other shows thrive according to
custom. If custom isn’t plentiful, they don’t
thrive much.
** * *
Barnum will be a showman till the Crack
of Doom. His latest masterpiece of “genial hum-
bug ” is an abortive attempt to have his grave
robbed.'
** * *
Janet Achurch was determined that if
Sydney people could not have Bernhardt they
should nevertheless have “ Camille,” and so that
unhealthy plant oped its sickly blooms at Sydney
Garrick contemporaneously with its Bernhardtian
blossoming forth at Melbourne Princess’. The
fair Janet is usually heavily handicapped by the
bad judgment displayed in casting her plays and
a lack of attention to detail, due no doubt to inept
stage-management. These weaknesses were, how-
ever, never more conspicuously in evidence than on
the first night of “ Camille,” when the fatuity of
the individual who cast Harry Power as the
Count de Varville—with a low comedy swagger
would have wrecked the play had not
the gallery been in very good humour. On
Monday night better counsel prevailed, and
Charles Charrington successfully doubled de
Varville and Duval pere, the latter being excel-
lently handled, though “made-up” all snowy
white bar the eyebrows, which were of raven black-
ness, an incongruity whose reasonableness is not
apparent. Probably, Janet Achurch’s Camille is
her best performance of the present season, and
worthy of a place alongside her Nora, Gilberte
and Fedora. It is free from the repellant throat-
gargling gurgles of her “Forget-rne-Not,” and is
only marred by occasional extravagances of
gesture, and some purposeless rushings up and
down stage, meant, possibly, to simulate terror,
but more suggestive of burlesque. Herbert
Flemming’s Armand is a carefully-studied,
highly dramatic and emotional character that
errs chiefly because stagey and over-demonstrative.
Armand, as we read him, was a dainty, highly-
strung man whose anger and jealousy couldn’t
have found vent in bluster and foot-stamping, who
would have stilletoed instead of bludgeoning the
objects of his wrath. But the tendency of the
whole performance is towards a boisterousness
and extravagance, which fatally blemish the
Prudence of Meta Pelham, otherwise happily
conceived. In certain lines Emma Bronton is a
capable actress, but though she looks nice enough
to eat, it is a physical impossibility for her to look
Nfimne, while her development makes her a bad foil
for Camille. Alice May makes a bright and chirpy
Nichette ; the Olympe of Miss Fortescue is credit-
able ; Charles Westrnacott’s Gustave is, perhaps,
too quiet only by contrast with the prevailing
noisiness; as Gaston, Harry Leston is all right in
an old part, nevertheless not quite in his vein. A
matinee benefit in aid of the Women’s College
Fund will take place on Saturday, when the
greater part of the stalls will be reserved and
Doll’s House ” will be played. “Asin a Look-
ing-Glass ” (the French version played by Bern-
hardt is named “Lena”), by F. C. Phillips, will
be played for the first time in Sydney on Saturday
evening. “ Devil Caresfoot,” Haddon Chambers’
earliest work, and “A Doll’s House,” thecourteous
Cunard informs us, are both underlined for pro-
duction, if necessary,
* -* * *
So far our prognostication of success for
“Jane” have been fully realised, for S.R.O. has
been hung out nightly and, unless “The Gon-
doliers ” knocks it endways, the season promises
to be the largest in the annals of Sydney
Criterion. It is very delicately handled at times.
Some of the broad lines are simply skated over so
swiftly that statements scarcely provoke a laugh
while, if emphasised, they might cause a cyclone
of cacchination, Brough, as “ the splendid liar ”
who proves his master, out of his mouth, to be
“ a bit of a liar ” himself, has embellished his
William considerably since opening-night, and
Arthur Lawrence’s Kershaw beams more and
more like a beatified Pickwick. The full signi-
ficance of Jane’s command to William —responsive
to the philanthropist’s remark that he in-
tends to enjoy himself in London—“ Hand
Mr. Kershaw a tart,” has so far been
lost to the simple-minded and unsuspicious
Sydney public ; but the veiled point penetrates
their perceptions, when Cecil Ward says, in
defence of his supposititious wife’s alleged desire
to keep secret the existence of her mythical off-
spring, “ Some women are like that, you know.”
They do know. Ward’s desire to borrow some
other man’s wife for 24 hours is also recognised as
a brilliant suggestion, and Pattie Browne makes a
hit when, in response to Kershaw’s question as to
her maternity, she invites him to inspect her
marriage-certificate of the same day’s date. Sug-
gestiveness, however, goes just a shade too far
when, in response to William’s suggestion that
they’ve forgotten the baby, he is requested by his
wife and her master to leave the room. The posi-
tion, however, is greedily swallowed by the very
people who strained at the gnat of Ibsenism.
Miss Romer, as the amorous and mortified
spi nster, is great in a thankless part, and Mrs. Bryer
as the mother who “ has forgotten the Foster”
though she apparently remembered the Guinness
scores heavily. “Jane ” should fill the bill until
the MacMahonian production of “ Evangeline,”
now running in Sandhurst and Ballarat, on
June 27.
** * *
Bland Holt, after three months’ big busi-
ness at Sydney Royal, has vamoosed to Adelaide
where he will continue his career of crime, carnage
and bloodshed. On Tuesday night the Opera
company opened in “ The Gondoliers,” the last
joint creation of Sullivan and Gilbert, with a cast
similar to that of its latest Melbourne perform-
ances, and differing only from the earlier ones in
that Elsie Cameron plays the Duchess of Plaza-
Toro —the part originally handled by Maggie
Moore; Henry Braoy, Marco, originally played
by Leumane while Violet Varley has succeeded Ida
Osborne as Tessa. The season promises to be one
of the biggest ever known at the Royal. Our
notice is unavoidably held over till next week.
** * *
The Jerseys and aides dropped into Syd-
ney Her Majesty’s on Monday night, in that
quiet, previously-unannounced manner which de-
prives the management of that opportunity for
the bold advertisement of gubernatorial patronage
that usually swells the treasury by the sparse
five-bobs of toadyism. After a period of great
anxiety, equine matters are now working smoothly,
and the smiles of James and Joseph MacMahon,
in conjunction with that of Frank J. Currier,
their stage-director, whose name hitherto we
have spelt as it is pronounced, fill the vestibule
with a radiance far surpassing that of incan-
descent lamps, shine they ever so brightly.
The brothers have in reserve a play named
“ The Still Alarm,” by Joseph Arthur,
which deals with conflagrations. One scene re-
presents the interior of a fire-brigade station as
the alarm rings, whereupon the horses run out of
their stalls to be harnessed by electricity, the fire-
men slide down the central pole and tumble into
their boots on the waggon, and the engine dashes
off O.P. inside eight seconds, the normal American
time in crack brigades. “Siberia” is doing very
good business, but “ The County Fair ” jumps the
claim on Saturday night, dead sure.

The event at Coogee Aquarium is said to
be the performance of a self-taught blue groper
who comes to the surface of a fish-tank and enter-
tains the audience with a dissertation on the
South American difficulty intoned in Barcelonian-
Belfast.

Last Thursday night the übiquitous
Wyburd gave one of his enjoyable smoke concerts
at Bondi Aquarium when about 150 members
rolled up to bo entertained by the tootling of the
Hungarian Cornet Quartette, Messrs. Fowler,
Tilley, M‘Mahon and von der Mehden and the
business of the clever Faust family. Another
“smoker ” takes place on Thursday week.

At Poverty Point.
Patti and Bernhardt have not loved money for its own
sake-it is only their artistic ambition to be paid higher
than anyone else, as a mark of pre-eminence ; wherefore,
when Patti and Nilsson were in the same company, Patti
was content to get guineas as against Nilsson’s pounds.
Other people have spent all the money of your Bernhardts
and Pattis.
Williamson and G. worked up the Bernhardt boom
without any placards—to the great disgust of the bill-
stickers.
How does Bernhardt stand amid the actresses of all
time ? Sara blends such opposites as Rachel and Mdlle.
Mars. She cuts up into Modjeska, Ellen Terry, and
half-a-dozen others. The Helen Faucit type is lofty, but
namby-pamby, for it seems that greatness must be in
wickedness, grand passion. All Sara’s great pieces wind
up with a death-scene, varied in Camille, Tosca,
Fedora, Ruy Bias, Theodora, Cleopatra, Frou Frou, Lena
(As in a Looking Glass), Adrienne Lecouvreur, and an
the rest, with a subtle art that never palls.
It would be possible for Bernhardt to give her pei-
formances in French, with an English-speaking company,
and perhaps some people would like it better, for the in-
congruity is by no means so great as may be imagined.
Rossi and Salvini, the Italian tragedians, went all o.ei
America with English-speaking troupes. Booth spose

Sara Bernhardt’s alternative, at 15, was “The Convent
or the Stage,” so she says. Turning up George Sands’
plays, one finds an am ising one, “Mirelle,” where the
Sara Bernhardt went to a nunnery, but, at 46 years old,
came in contact with the players, and is introduced
behind the scenes, with odd contrasts.

Sara Bernhardt’s Sydney Town Hall reception should
have been delayed, in the interests of Abbey and W. and
G., until just prior to her Sydney opening. It was a
funereal affair, somewhat relieved by the organ playing of
Mr. Sykes, the talented Huddersfieldian who, thanks to
the resignation of the touring Burdekin, was permitted to
give a taste of his quality'. By the way, why did the
S.M. Herald, a paper that went out of its way to eulogise
a late use of Sydney’s organ, as a mere instrument of
accompaniment, omit all mention of Mr. Sykes’ organ-
recital at Sydney University ?
there are rumours in London of a coolness between the
divine Ellen Terry and the ricketty Henry. A rival
Richard is in the field, ’tis said, hence the tears. Ellen,
whom age cannot wither nor custom stale, was never
noted for constancy', as parish registers and the archives
of the law-courts can testify. The name of the gent has
not transpired—or is it gents?

The following, from a London correspondent by last
mail, will now have to be read in connection with the
attack made since the mail left by Phil Robinson (editor
of Augustus Harris’ paper London Sunday Times, without
the knowledge or consent of his employer) upon Tummy:
“ Augustus Harris is likely to achieve the summit of his
ambition, for it is arranged that the Emperor of Germany
will pay a visit to the City when he comes over to Eng-
land in July. This will mean, of course, according to
custom, a baronetcy for the Lord Mayor, and knighthood
for the Sheriffs. Gus, is big enough as it is. but Drury
Lane won’t be able to hold him as ‘Sir Gus., Bart.’ He
generally drives to the theatre in his official Coach, with
gold-laced flunkeys in attendance. This is considered
bad form by City magnates, but what cares he ? It is a
good ad., and that is all he thinks of.” How furious Harris
must be over the Phil Robinson affair !
Theatrical managers are notoriously disloyal personages
in private* as regards mere political loyally, but nobody
has to put on the kootoo so much, for business reasons,
Obvious. Look now at Gusty Harris, owner of Madame
Midas’ well-corned Phil. Robinsonian paper, Sunday
Times, In which to ’Arris’s Jenkinsonian ’error, Phil., put a
reference to the notorious Sandringham mortgage. Im-
perial Wales summons Harris, crawling on his stomach, to
the Mawlburrow, and poor graceless Phil’s sacked. Fred.
Lyster ruined himself in Australia by incautious American
talk, in a Ballarat bar, about Clontarf.
Young Masherton, on the stage, complained that Props
didn’t give him real chicken, for a stage-supper. “ All
right,” says Props, “ you’re poisoned in the last act, and
shall have the real thing.”
While Irving was acting Hamlet, the Chinese Am-
bassador, and staff, in full regalia, visited behind the
scenes. They missed their way, and the whole procession
was only just stopped from marching on to the stage, in
the plaj'-scene, an effect which might even have been
instructive to Shakespeare.
London critics, who are all dramatists, find Bulwer’s
“Money,” as revived at the Vaudeville, very poor stuff
nowadays, but one would like to see the Jones, Pinero,
Grundy, or Chambers, who can ’ turn out another
“ Money.”

Barnum’s will covers 100 pages Of legal foolscap. He
left £1,000,000, of which his wife receives £22,000 ab-
solutely and £B,OOO a year. The rest goes in bequests to
various institutions.
In Barnum’s troubled days, when he proposed to buy
the American museum, a friend who was aware of the
State of his finances said, “You buy the American
iiduseuiii! With what?” “ Brass,” replied Barnum, “for
gold and silver have I none.”

When General Tom Thumb was arranging to marry
Lavinia Warren, the dwarf, in face of the ferocious
jealousy of Commodore Nutt, who once knocked the
General down, some mischievous young ladies concealed
themselves and overheard the General’s proposal. He
began in a very business-like manner by producing an
enormous policy of insurance upon his property and
ended by puling his arm round her waist and kissing
her.

oay, it certain rumours current about himself and Ellen
Terry are not true.
Two adventurous ladies have ventured on the experi-
ment of producing Ibsen’s “ Hedda Gabler”at London
Vaudeville, and have made a decided score, says a cor-
respondent. No play of Ibsen’s has been so well acted in
London. Miss Robins, as Hedda, is a big success, and
Miss Marion Lea, as Mrs. Elvsted, quite carries the audi-
ence away. A few more such performances and there
would be hope for Ibsen on the English stage.
It is said that Mrs. Langtry is many thousands of
pounds poorer by her venture in management. It was
announced just before the mail left that she had accepted
an engagement with Gus. Harris for Drury Lane at a
salary of £l5O per week. People may believe as much as
they like about the muchness of the salary.
Wilson Barrett, says our London correspondent, has
put up at the New Olympic a version of the time-honoured
“Belphegor,” which he calls “ The Acrobat.” It is beau-
tifully mounted and dressed. Wilson is also going to
inflict upon a suffering British public a series of matinees
of “ Hamlet.”

W. and G. were quite sentimental in their advt. of
Ryley’s benefit at Melbourne Princess's. “ Within a few
months his talents as an artist, and his amiable and gentle-
manly qualities as a man, have won for him a foremost
position in the esteem of the press, the public, and of his
coadjutors in the theatre,” ran the managerial note.
Wherefore the firm “tendered Mr. Ryley a complimentary
benefit as a testimonial of our appreciation.” They
couldn’t have expressed themselves more heartily if the
baritone had been an old and valued head of a counting-
house, but Ryley deserves well of the perfesh, inasmuch
as he has proved that good-fellowship is not an un-
known quantity in the market-value of a good actor.
Everybody seems to like Ryley, who carries a card of in-
troduction in his face, which is happy and wholesome
rather than theatrically handsome. According to rumour,
too, there are several husbands and fathers deeply in-
debted to the young man for his stern refusal to take up
the part of Don Giovanni when it was pressed upon him.
Good old Ryley!

the best on earth.” We’ve all met him.

Charles Warner has been playing his old part in “ It’s
