Flora Graupner has a little way of pretending to burst
out laughing at the whispered jests, or interpolated en-
dearments of her stage-lovers. It looks wondrous fresh
and natural until you have become used to it.
Johnny Toole to Paris correspondent of the London
D.T., on the subject of his travels, is very instructive
reading. Such trivial matters as bad business, or luke-
warm notices, were graciously overlooked in the J.L.T.
narrative, though he came out strong on the public recep-
tions, hospitality, floral tributes and vice-regal compli-
ments. “But to return to the Governors,” said the little
man, after making some presumably “ funny ” comments
on the fact that his name was printed in bigger type than
those of “the chief members of my loyal company who
shared these triumphs with me.” To return to the
Governors ! It recalls to mind the old yarn told of
Ducrot, who hadn’t patience to listen to the dialogue of
his hippodromioal dramas, but would say at rehearsal—
“ Let’s get to the ’osses.”

The antithesis of Mrs. Potter.
According to a celebrated actor, a man may smile and
smile and be a Willard. “Indeed,” says the original
Cyrus Blenkarn, “ an habitual villain on the stage is, as
far as my experience goes, exactly the opposite off the
stage. Take, for example, Mr. 0. Smith, of the Adelphi
Theatre in London, a celebrated villain of the beetle-
browed and russet-booted and belted and buckled type.
Why, off the stage that man kept white mice and canary
birds, and was never known to say a harsh word to any-
body.” But then The Bulletin remembers that Peace, the
burglar, kept canaries—and what price Count Tosco, the
dainty bird-fancier of “The Woman in White,” as an
oleaginous and smooth-spoken villain?

guineas.
As Jennie Lee walked along George-street, Sydney, on®
day, a wretched hoodlum standing on the Post Office step B
howled out to her, “’Ere, missus, give us half’’—alluding
to a bag of grapes Jennie was just then carrying. To the
amazement of this miserable kid she turned towards him,
borrowed her companion’s penknife, severed half the
bunch and presented it to the youth, who did his best at
an apology for his rudeness.

Someone writes to a N.Z. weekly to say, apropos to a
local amateur Macbeth, who wore tweed trousers, that—
“ The Scandinavians assuredly wore trousers. In one of
the Sagas a man is mentioned as cutting a hole in the seat
of his trousers, putting his head through the opening,
and so wearing them as a cloak to protect him from the
cold.”
While the Dobson-Kennedy Company were playing
“ The Shaughraun ” at Foxton, in N.Z., a drunken man
took possession of the stage. Everyone seemed paralysed
till Kennedy, playing Conn., arose from his death-bed
and, discarding his Winding-sheet, threw the beery man
but with a loud splash into the mud slough locally desig-
nated a street. He then lapsed calmly into his death-
trance, and the wake went on.
Someone writes to S.M. Herald complaining that the
Australian gallery is noisier than that of Europe. Per-
haps ; but for noise, Catcalling, interjection, and idiotic
yelling it is an Eden compared to the gallery of London,
Birmingham, Leeds, Manchester, Liverpool, Dublin,
Edinburgh, or Glasgow. Ask any travelled pro.
The Dampier-Carver “Scout” is not an original idea.
At Manchester New Queen’s Theatre, a play called “On
the Frontier ” was lately running, in which all the prairie
business, down to the Custer massacre, was exemplified.
Granville introduced Regnier to Gladstone. The states-
man at once asked the actor whether he did not miss in
England “the impressive gesticulation which comes so
naturally to your countrymen ?” Regnier replied that on
the contrary he had been struck with the excessive
gesticulation of English actors. One of the first things
taught in the French Conservatoire is to be sparing of
all movements of the head and hands; indeed, learners
often had their hands tied to their sides.
Recent events remind us of an old scandal. An entre-
preneur stole away the heart of an actress from its tem-
Eorary holder, a composing conductor, who, in revenge,
olted with the manager’s prima donna. Then a certain
bashful, blushing journalist appeared on the scene, and
took the actress on tour on strength of the managerial
diamonds and furniture. All the dramatis personce are
now in Australia, bar the conductor and singer, but the
latter has hankerings after a professional visit.

Tragedian O’Connor thus recounts a recent experience
on tour in Pennsylvania The first night, sir, I was
hissed-hissed, sir ; the second night, I was egged, sir-
egged: but the third night, sir”—and the tragedian
thumped his chest—“the third night, sir, I played behind
a net.”

invited in his official capacity and refused in his official

The screen-scene, “School for Scandal,” has been
fruitful in incidents. When the Earl of Derby courted
Miss Farren, and she acted Lady Teazle, he used to go
round from the dress-circle, and talk with her behind the
screen, so that many a time a mischievous upset was
planned. Another lady, acting Lady Teazle, slipped from
behind the screen to the green-room, and was not on the
spot when Chas. Surface turned the screen over. Once,
when Mrs. G. B. W. Lewis played Lady Teazle, she was
found in a faint when the screen came down.

In Pinero’s new “ Lady Bountiful” there is a remark-
ably clever suggestion of a scene not displayed. It is the
ante-chamber of a fashionable ladies’ riding-school, from
whence you hear the thud of the ’orses’ ’oofs on the tan,
the tinkle of stirrups, and clank of bits, in the most
natural way, but the best suggestion ever staged was the
roar of the advancing mob, in Ristori’s “Marie Antoinette.”
When young Dumas handed the MSS. of “ Camille ” to
his father, the old man was so delighted that he wept.
Camille (Marguerite Gauthier) was a real person, and
herein is the subtle power of the drama—in one way
perhaps more fresh and vivid on the stage than any
other 40 years old.

Government party arose from the fact that
Haynes had been touring N.S.W. and had just
seen in twenty constituencies that the Common-
wealth Bill would not go down at any price.

how the cat was jumping, so he went volte-face

James Swan, M.L.C., of Queensland, died
the other day on board the steamer Jelunga, in
the Mediterranean Sea, while en voyage to Eng-
land. He was 80 years old. He came out to
Sydney, in 1837, as a printer, at the instance of
Dr. Lang. In ’46 he joined Brisbane Courier,
which he presently purchased, remaining its sole
proprietor till ’59, and fervently following Dr.
Lang’s patriotic lead in the anti-transportation
movement, besides championing the cause of
Moreton Bay separation and the autonomous
government of Queensland. He was three times
Mayor of Brisbane and had been a nominee legis-
lator for thirteen years.
The leaders of the various Australian
Governments have decided to discard the title
“Premier” and adopt the more Parkesianly
grandiloquent term “Prime Minister.” Asa
matter of strict Parliamentary nomenclature,
there is no such term as “Premier” known to
English State papers, as Sir Samuel Griffith once
laboriously pointed out to the Queensland Parlia-
ment, and many would not be surprised to learn
that the change is due largely to Sam’s finical
fastidiousness. By common usage, the word
“Premier” has acquired an Australian signifi-
cance not easy to rub out; so “ Premier ”it will,
The Bulletin predicts, always be. Short and
un-pompous.
The prospectus of the Sydney Deposit
Bank, whose director is Mr. T. S. Richardson, lies
before us. This institution has introduced a
novelty for public use in the shape of deposit-
notes, which can be purchased by anyone without
giving name or address, while translerrable with-
out endorsement, therefore can be paid away dr
given as security, while bearing interest at a high
rate during the time of currency. These notes are
issued from £lO upwards for any term at the fol-
lowing rates : 12 months, 7 per cent. ; 6 months, 6
par cent. ; 3 months, 5 percent. But special rates
can be procured for longer or shorter periods.
The Bank on fixed deposits allows for 3 years, OJ>
per cent. ; 12 months, 83 per cent. ; 6 months, 7|
per cent.; 3 months, 5J per cent., and during the
last four years a bonus of 1 per cent, has been paid
to all depositors in addition to the current rates on
their deposits. This is the only Bank in Austra-
lasia that thus divides its profits.
That Queen Victoria should desire to con-
fer with Sir George Grey, and that Zealandia*s
G.O.M. should resolve to visit England at her
call, does not surprise those who are aware of the
confidential and almost affectionate relations that
have long existed between the Conservative
Sovereign and the Democratic statesman. For
over a score of years the Queen has treated Grey
as if he was a Privy Councillor, sending him holo-
graph letters and telegrams, asking his advice on
affairs of State, and confiding to him her private
opinions regarding many important matters.
These documents are in the hands of a third
person, but will not, of course, be published
until the Queen and the present generation of
politicians shall have passed away, when they will
no doubt form interesting reading. The Auckland
Observer (edited, by the way, by the poet Kelly,
who, in addition to writing a panegyric on Grey,
took the palm for a poem on the Queen’s Jubilee,
opines that the Queen’s object in calling the Great
Pro-Consul to London is to elevate him to the
Peerage. That may be so. The offer of a seat in
the House of Lords will be a severe trial of Grey’s
Democratic principles. But perhaps Victoria
only wants to ask him if he will be the first
Governor-General of Federated Australia. What
a lark that would be!
The facts in the sensational nullity-of 7
marriage case, Tyson v. Tyson (or Logan), just
heard in Sydney before Judge Windeyer : James
Tyson the younger (a nephew of Millionaire
Jimmy) was married at St. Stephen’s (C. of E,),
Richmond River, in ’Bl, to Harriet Logan, whom
he believed to be a widow ; subsequently he dis-
covered that Logan, the husband, did not die till
’B5, so that his marriage was invalid. Thus far,
the allegations were not disputed on either side,
In November, ’B6, the pair attended the R.C.
church at Hay, N.S.W., and underwent some
ceremony celebrated by the Rev. Father Kiley.
Tyson declared that this ceremony was a harmless
bit of mummery (in Latin) performed to please
Harriet, who feared she was near death, and
wanted to get “the blessing of the Church”
on their former Protestant marriage. Harriet
and Father Kiley, on the other hand, swore
positively that “ the regular marriage ceremony ”
(in English) of the R.C. Church was celebrated
on that occasion, and with Tyson’s full knowledge
and consent, after he had voluntarily consented to
and had been administered “conditional baptism
and conditional absolution.” Father Kiley further
asserted that nuns were present in an adjacent
oratory behind a lattice, and Harriet said she
heard the nuns joining in the marriage-service
responses. Father Kiley, however, made the
strange and startling admission that he got no
declaration in writing from Tyson, and that,
during his 17 years of priesthood, he had taken
declarations from all other parties he had married.
A spice of piquancy was added to the case by
accusations made by Harriet against James of his
intimacy with a governess, but these allegations
were emphatically denied both by James and the
governess. At the time of writing, judgment is
reserved.

TWO OUT OF TWO HUNDRED.
A ROUNDABOUT REVIEW.
How vain a creature is Man, even under
the most favourable conditions for being humble !
I low much vainer when he is a Poet —a Maker !
. . . On the day that Henry Kendall was
buried at Waverley by the sea a certain disciple
of his, to whom the deceased singer was popularly
supposed to have left his mantle, met a minor
bard, and, grasping him earnestly by the hand,
said, in elegiac tones, “Alas! poor Kendall is
gone ! There’s only you and me left now !” Left
10 write poetry, he meant. No greater mistake
was ever made by any waker of the living lyre.
Poets are as plentiful as rabbits in Australia at the
present date of writing. That eminent political
chiffonier, Douglas Brooke Wheelton Sladen, says
in the preface to his last volume of collections,
that he is personally acquainted with the works
of over two hundred (200) Australian poets ! The
idea is almost too gigantic to be grasped by the
ordinary mind, but Mr. Sladen makes light of it.
“ Only two-hundred poets in all Australia !” he
seems to say, “too bad!” And he calls for
more !
# # *
Of course there are more than two hun-
dred. At the right season of the year you will
find—figuratively speaking—a poet under every
wattle tree weeping, because somebody (it
doesn’t matter who so Aug as he is dead) beneath
its golden bloom is calmly sleeping. Most poets,
indeed, take a gloomy view of life, which is
perhaps natural under the circumstances. Besides,
it is easier to write lugubriously then to write
tightly. Almost anybody can bo maudlin. And
then the rhymes come so readily and are so sug-
gestive—weep, sleep, grave so deep, worms that
creep, and so forth. The dismal little poems in
y. Inch these bards do most delight generally appear
in the quiet corners of country newspapers (re-
minding one of the dank little graves, overgrown
with sodden flowers, one sometimes sees in the
corners of old cemeteries) when the patent-medi-
cine advertisement, which has not brought in any
money for years, is too large or too small to fill the
space left open by a temporary stagnation in
police-court business. They harm nobody, and,
once published, are never afterwards heard of, until
some defiler of graves, like the aforesaid Sladen,
with unhallowed shovel digs them up and inde-
cently exposes them to the world.
=5- : # s*.
Yet, if by these means the world is made
to realise that there is such a thing as an Austra-
lian literature in process of growth, and to become
enthusiastic over the discovery, who will blame
Mr. Sladen and his kind for their conduct ? No
member of the Australian Natives’ Association, at
any rate, we should hope. For it is a fact that
there is a demand for Australian literature in
London just now, and the present writer is
acquainted with two or three ambitious young
men who are working night and day with a" view
t 3 supptying that demand as soon as they can
make arrangements to travel as ocean-steamer
stewards’ assistants and interview the heads of
the great publishing firms as man to man. But,
then, young men are all writers of stories—mere
Australian Eudyard Kiplings. Since Mr. Francis
Adams went to London no poet from Australia
has arrived there in bodily substance, as far as we
know—unless he travelled incognito, which is an
unnatural supposition. This is a pity, because the
others, the story-writers, being to a man sordid
grubbers for coin—a reproach which, for obvious
reasons, can never be made against the poets —are
no great credit to Australia, however successful
they may turn out to be. They have their reward.
They grow rich and “rest on their oars at San
Eemo,”as Grant Allen said (in the AEGUS)Fergus
Hume —the author of that jerry-built narrative
of the MurderAVill-Out Series, “The Mystery of
a Hansom Cab ” —was doing when the last mail
left Europe. But the poets have their reward,
too. It is in the consciousness that they have, of
their,own free will, chosen the nobler part. What
is Coin compared with Immortality? Nothing—
to a poet. They also have their peculiar pleasures.
How delightful, for instance, it must be to a
Bard, when a friend or two are'- spending the
evening with him and the talk turns, as it always
does on such occasions, to high, intellectual
themes to be able to say—rising and placing his
finger on a thin, chastely-bound book standing
between Shakespeare and Tennyson in his book-
case —“I think the idea you refer to is rather
delicately expressed in a short poem I dashed off
when I was sick that time ; It is here in my last
volume. I will send it to you !” But how much
more delightful, when the Bard is young, and
before his hair has begun to fall out, to be able to
pour his honeyed rhymes into the delicately-
freckled ears of the Beloved while she urgeth him
t > continue even as did the maiden in Gifted
Hopkins’ poem—
“ 0 poet, read !—read all thy wondrous scroll!
Vea, read the verse that maketh glad to hear !”
Then I began and read two sweet, brief hours,
And she forgot all love save only mine !
* * ‘ #
Two volumes of verse are now lying on the
table in front of us—one in dead-blue binding with
the title in gilt-lettering ; the other in lavender-
grey with gilt title; both published in London.
Let ns begin with the dead-bine book. The
author, Mr. George Essex Evans, calls it “The
Eepentanoe of Magdalene Despar and other
I’oems.” We have conscientiously read it through
from end to end, under the impression that such
an elegant casket must surely contain some jewels
cf value. We found some, but they were gems,
for the most part, that had been taken out of their
original settings and remounted in an inferior
stylo of art. As thus :
Ah ! I doubt not that hereafter we shall pass from change
to change,
All the spirit growing finer, all the thought with wider
range.
T 1 iis is how the idea appears in “Locksley
Hall”:-
And, I doubt not, through the ages an increasing purpose
runs,
And the thoughts of men are widened with the process of
the suns.
Curious similarity in the metre, too. But to do
Mr. Evans justice, he expands and spiritualises
the idea he borrows from Tennyson. Again
And what is life, and what is man,
Without that freshness of the heart
Which once was ours, but never can
Return when youth and faith depart ?
Times give us much, but who will say—
Js much as all it takes away.
—Evans.
There’s not a joy the Earth can give like that it takes
away
When the glow of early youth is lost in feeling’s dull
decay.
Byron.
Evans fills more space, but we think it
will be admitted that Byron puts the thought
more pointedly. Probably he spoke from large
knowledge of the subject. Besides, he spoke first.
Another instance. Mr. Evans, in a short poem
entitled “Sailing,” says :
When you raised your voice to sing
In a key so strong and true,
E’en the sea-birds on the wing
Seemed to pause and list to you.
This is a charming fancy. Longfellow set it some-
what differently. After telling how the noble
Count Arnaldos saw a fair and stately galley sail-
ing inward to the land, the elder poet said :
And he heard the ancient helmsman
Sing a song so wild and clear
That the sailing sea-bird slowly
Paused upon the mast to hear.
There are many more curious coincidences of this
kind scattered through the volume, but these will
suffice to show that Mr. Evans has been a diligent
student of the poetry of the present century at
any rate. His book is indeed, to a large extent,
a book of echoes.
APPLYING THE ADJECTIVE.
S.M.: “What is your occupation ? ”
TOUGH ’UN ; “I’m a labourer, yer washup.”
S.M.: “I see; one of the hard labourers, I presume? One month without the option.”
We resolved to see if we couldn’t find
some striking and original idea by closing the
volume suddenly and opening it at random. This
is what we found in a poem entitled “The Shep-
herd’s Last Sleep ” :
In the lonely bush in a far-off land,
Where the wattles bloom and the brigalows wave ;
Laid to his rest by a stranger’s hand,
The exile sleeps in his nameless grave.
Ah, yes, the deceased shepherd ! We know him
well: he is the King Charles’ head of Australian
poets. Their muse may be grave or gay, lively or
severe, but she never fails to bring her little post-
mortem tribute to the tomb of the departed herder
of sheep. At times the ludicrous fancy seizes us
that they are all weeping over the same
grave, and are all shareholders in the moulder-
ing remains below. But this is impossible.
The shepherd never lived ; he is only a property-
shepherd. Those poetic immortelles you arc
hanging on the wattle that droops over his alleged
burial-mound are simply elegiac exercises. The
shepherd whose pretended loss they mourn is as
unlike the shepherd of real life—who smokes,
chews, swears frightfully and has a fit of delirium
tremens every time he visits the township with a
cheque—as the latter is unlike a shepherd in
Dresden China.
* * *
Let us turn to the poem which gives a title
to the volume—“ Magdalene Despar.” The story
told in this is tragic, but somewhat silly. Mag-
dalene marries an old man, and, after due con-
sideration, deceives him (as is generally the case
under such circumstances). He discovers this
(which is not invariably the case) and, crazy with
grief, enlists in the Soudan Contingent- as well
as we can make out—and is, very properly, killed
off early in the campaign. His friends, however,
are comforted by learning that
His shroud was the flag ho defended
His bier was a mound of the slain !
A sentiment of the shambles ! We can imagine
the afflicted hero of a poem getting some conso-
lation out of cutting his own throat, but for the
life of us we can’t understand what solace he can
find in cutting the throats of black strangers.
And, anyhow, if facts count for anything, the
contingent confined itself exclusively to cutting
the throats of goats and the leading-strings of
However, Magdalene, as soon as she hears that
her husband is dead, walks, if you please—very
deliberately into the sea till she gets out of her
depth and is drowned in due course. This is sup-
posed to be her repentance. The poem winds up
with the following lines :
Hut the night-breeze, blowing shoreward, bore a sound
o’er vale and hollow
“ Magdaleni ! Magdalen 6 !” ever calling from the sea,
And the trembling echo answered, “Onward! Onward;
I will follow
Wheresoe’er thy voice shall lead me till it lead at last to
Thee.”
The voice is the voice of her dead child, and it
leads her to suicide ! The moral is bad enough—
though we don’t mind that so much —but the art
is shocking. There is a suggestion of Brunton
Stephens’ “Convict Once” in “Magdalene
Despar,” which is unfortunate for the latter. The
contrast is as fatal to it as that with a strong
colour always is to a weak one.
# # #
Mr., Evans, as becomes his Welsh name,
is a warlike bard. His Muse is no serene and
graceful Greek maiden, but a wild-horse Val-
kyrie—a Chooser of the Slain. Gentler minstrels
may sing softly sweet in Lydian measures, but
for him there is no music to compare with the
clatter of half-a ton of old iron as it comes crash-
ing to the ground with a wicked knight inside it,
cloven to the chin by the battle-axe of the good
knight as he rides amok through the melee cracking
skulls like eggs, and making a bloody omelette of
his foes. Very fine, no doubt —these old, mediaeval
heavyweights were good men in their day and
way, but what the devil brings them into this
gallery ? It is with the flashing cheque-book a”" 1
not the flashing sword that the spurs of knight-
hood are Avon in these times. This by the way,
however. It was quite natural that a bard
breathing such a spirit of slaughter should have
written an ode on the departure of the Soudan
Contingent, and not surprising that the ode
should have been stuffed with fustian like the
following :
When furious and fast the battel runs,
Australia’s eyes will watch her soldier sons ;
When through the haze
Of battle-clouds the dusky hordes appear
Australia’s sons will hold her honour dear.
Should glory’s rays
Shine where the southern banners proudly wave,
Then not in vain her chivalry she gave !
Reads like grim irony now, doesn’t it? “Love’s
Conquest ” is a long allegorical poem, also full of
fighting, in which one of the champions comes
forward and says
My name is Love, they call me the Sublime.
The first idea that struck us on reading this line
was that the champion had made a mistake in
giving his name and should have said:—
My name is Burke, they call me the Sublime.
But this is not sober criticism. The last poem in
the volume is called “ The Sorrel Mare,” and is a
sorry imitation of Gordon’s “ Britomarte.” Here
are a few specimens the italics are ours :
But leading his comrades by half a field
Steadily onwards the gray horse stealed.
Like clockwork she galloped, like lightning flew.
Like clockwork! Poor! Why not “like one
o’clock ?”
But straight ahead looms a bullfinch fence,
Black and gaunt with a stiff oak rail;
He steadies the mare ; she knows his sense—
Why, this is the most spavined stuff we have read
for years. Mr. Evans’ Pegasus suffers too much
from string-halt and staggers to be taken out for a
ride after Adam Lindsey Gordon. If there were
a Society for Prevention of Cruelty to Poetic
Animals, he (Mr, Evans) would probably be
prosecuted for having made the attempt.
Mr. Evans is happier in dealing witli
purely Australian themes. Here is a very good
picture in verse :
The purple hills rise far behind,
Before me spreads the plain,
The tall grass shakes beneath the wind
Like surges on the main.
Thin mists have girt each low hill’s crest,
The hot sun swims in cloudless blue,
A mirage gathers in the West
And trembles into view ;
It gathers in the swimming haze,
A silver lake of dazzling sheen,
Its waves are bright with dancing light,
And tender tints of blue and green.
If there were more of this sort of work in the hun-
dred and fifty pages which make up Mr. Evans’
volume, it might be considered a welcome addition
to Australian poetry. But his style is as knobbly
and uneven as a sack of coals. The lines “to the
Author of ‘ Morna Lee ’ ” (whoever he or she may
be) might, with reservations, have been written of
his own verse :
a mystic song
Far-echoing thro’ the Halls of Thought
With weird vibrations that belong
To the Unsought.
And uncalled-for, we might add.
* * *
Now for the volume in lavender-grey. We
have here to deal with a lady. Mrs. James
Glenny Wilson, otherwise known in literature as
“Austral,” is the authoress of these “Themes
and Variations.” If she had called them “Dis-
tillations from well-known Poets ” the title would
have been more accurately descriptive of the con-
tents, which consist of verses that might have
been written by almost any person with poetic
feeling (a quality by no means uncommon) a good
knowledge of English poetry, and current thought,
a delicate ear, and an eye for the picturesque.
There are pretty gifts and accomplishments, but
it takes more than those to constitute the poet —
nascitur non 'fit. But Mrs. Wilson makes amends
for the want of candour in her title by the follow-
ing graceful and witty lines in her preface :
To you, chief poets of the elder times,
We have much to forgive ! For still, I fear
You have cribbed all the fairest of our rhymes
Ere yet we waked to see the sunrise clear.
The concluding lines are worthy of Hood in his
serious moments :
And yet it may be that we sometimes glean
In other’s fields, although unconsciously ;
We are sleep-walkers and our feet have been
We know not when, beneath our mother sky,
Light moves his step, who walks the waving rye !
The moon is large—the rustling harvest gleams,
Soft music stirs—wake not the fool of dreams !
Vet even this apology for unconscious plagiarism is
in itself a plagiarism which, if intentional, is a
rare piece of art. The idea appears in the second
introductory sonnet to Kendall’s “Leaves from
Australian Forests,” wherein the poet says
I would not do the lordly masters wrong, •
By filching fair words from the shining throng,
Whose music haunts me as the wind a tree"!
Lo ! when a stranger, in soft Syrian glooms
Shot through with sunset, treads the cedar delh
And hears the breezy ring of elfin bells
Far down by where the white-haired cataract booms,
He, faint with sweetness caught from forest spells,
Bears thence, unwitting, plunder of perfumes.
Mrs. Wilson has a delicate sympathy with
Nature in her calmer moods. These poems are
all of the Gentle Life. She has considered the
trees and the flowers how they grow, and is,
indeed, at her best when she is making little
water-colour sketches, in verse, of what the artists
call “good bits ”of scenery. As for example
A little house, o’erhung with flowers,
Where first the creamy primrose breaks,
Where roses tell the summer hours
With bursting buds and falling flakes.
A little garden, blossom-starred,
With jewelled fruit and honeyed bell,
With holly for its prickly guard,
And bee for humming sentinel.
A little stream that sings and frets,
And hurries onward to the main,
Who drinks of it, though worlds divide,
Will surely come and drink again.
This is dainty work, and seems to proceed n-
naturally from the pen of a lady as point-lace
from her needle. Here is something in a deeper
key :
The day was passing in a stillness deep,
When not the lightest air would lift its wing,
And Winter seemed to turn, in his white, dream-haunted
sleep,
Half-awakened by the coming steps of Spring.
A pallid sky, unwarmed by sunset’s glow,
Fold after fold of rippling cloud outspread,
And sent from the reddening west the lazy cry of the
crow
Seemed sinking into the stillness overhead.
And an inarticulate whisper far away
Rolled from the woods, and met the whispering main *
So strange it seems to me to live to-day,
1 cannot think it strange to live again.
We have all had this feeling at some time or other,
and an eerie unearthly feeling it is, too. Mrs,
Wilson heads the stanzas quoted “ A Hope.” A
faint hope, indeed, yet better than none at all.
Here are some fine lines on “ Miracles ” :
Half of our nature lies beneath the deep ;
And sometimes, as we measure out the fields,
There comes a murmur from the rooted hills.
A tremor shakes our island to its heart,
And strikes us dumb ; and all the flowery land
Looks strange ; and pale the autumnal-golden sun.
We know well the music of this metre, with its
falling cadences, and it has always seemed to us
that, as in the case of the Byronic stanza, it com-
pelled the writer who used it to follow in the foot-
steps, and think over again the thoughts of its
first great master, who has himself said in regard
to imitation that “ all can grow the flower
when all have got the seed. ” He might with more
accuracy have written “ all must grow the flower
when all have got the seed.” It is a very dangerous
thing to meddle with metres over which the
Masters of Song hold mortgages. Simple quatrains
or couplets are always safe because they are as old
as rhyme itself, and therefore the peculiar pro-
perty of no poet, however great. Talking of
imitations, what is to be thought of the following:

My friends were gone. There was no helper nigh,
The angel raised his visor at my cry.
I feared to see the face of Death—but he
Smiled, and I saw Love waiting there for me.
Anyone who will turn to Elizabeth Barrett
Browning’s “Sonnets from the Portuguese” will
find the same idea in a sonnet ending with the
line—we have not Mrs. Browning’s poems at hand,
and therefore quote from memory—
And swift the answer came : “ Not Death, but Love.”
# # *
Mrs. Wilson is, to say truth, a delightful
imitator, whether unconsciously or otherwise.
What, for instance, could be more charming than
the following “Imitation of a Swedish Song ” :
When Winter drives her flood abroad
And fleeces all the vale,
We bar the door, it is so chill—
So chill in Aladale.
When Spring sails in her merchantmen,
Full-loaded to the rail
With flower and leaf, it is so green—
So green in Aladale.
When Summer builds a cloudy tower,
And corn is golden-pale,
We bind the harvest-sheaves with joy—
With joy in Aladale !
When Autumn rains our apples down
And sings a wild sea-tale,
Thou with the swallows must depart—
Far, far from Aladale !
This has all the qualities of a song, which
should be limpid as a running brook and, like a
b o ik, carry along its own time. We must draw
H a close quickly. Mrs. Wilson’s poems are
graceful, thoughtful, tender, but they produce in
us that most painful of mental sensations —the
try'ng to remember where one has seen something
similar before. One is not so affected by Mr.
Evans’ poems ; One remembers at once without
trying. Both writers have sampled largely the
wine of recent song ; but while Mrs. Wilson pre-
se-ves only the bouquet of the various brands,
Mr. Evans' keeps only flip lees. There is, how-
ever, with all his crudeness, smie hope of the
latter writing something worthy of being remem-
bered. lie must, however, first learn not to look
upon poetry as a business. Let him, when he
has a good idea, put it into rhyme, if he must,
but all ideas are not to be used in this way, though
no less a poet than Words worth has set the perni-
cious example of looking upon every incident of
life, however trivial, as poetry in the raw. _ We
can fancy a poet of this kind cross-examining
himself at night in something like the following
style—“ Have any subjects for poems been
suggested by the events of the day? Yes —The
Cutting off of the Gas : Mem.—Hymn : “ The
Light of Heaven is Free.” The Dead Cat in the
Right-of-way: Mem.—Elegy : “In Striped and
Spotted Beauty Dead.” And so on. We have
done now.

Nicholas Heeney, who was murdered,
apparently with a tomahawk, the other day, on
Soldier’s Hill, Ballarat, Vic., by some unknown
assassin, was educated for the Church, but aban-
doned a religious vocation when the Australian
goldfields broke out. He had one fixed idea—viz.,
that his death would be by murder.
When Count de Morny died, his beautiful
Russian widow cut off her magnificent golden
tresses close to lier liGcid, cind put them in the
coffin, but they had grown a little when, three
years after, she married again, the Due de Sisos—
not scissors.

An autograph letter of Thackeray’s, re-
cently sold, reveals the queer fact that he once
dreamed of getting a billet as Assistant-Secretary
in the London G.P.O. Certainly, W. M. T.s
humble disciple, Anthony Trollope, was a G.P.O.
clerk, and picked up his best characters in the
office.

Terribly cruel were the circumstances
under which the Rev. S. Knight (Melb.) heard the
news of the death of his accomplished daughter,
Dr. Adela Knight, M.D., in Vienna, Just as he
was going to church, to preach, on Sunday even-
ing, came a telegram about her burial, without a
word of her death :—“ Shall I arrange grave-
stone ?”
The Duchess of Fife’s baby is to be
christened not Mary Ann Fife, or anything of
that sort, but Alexandra, after the Princess of
Wales. This is an interesting fact to cable oyer,
because we’ve been wondering for some time
whether baby would be named after its grand-
mother, or one of its aunts, or whether V.R. and I.
would order it to be simply numbered in the
Royal Family stud-book.

"Old Tecumseh.”
“He was in nothing an imitator.” So writes
President Harrison of one of the greatest and one
of the most picturesque of the great characters
developed by the civil war. The peculiar feature
of General Sherman’s character was his piquant
originality. He was a thorough master of the
developed science of warfare, but he built the
solid structure of his enduring fame as a great
soldier upon a basis of entire originality. He did
his own thinking, and so completely at times was
he at variance with those about him that he was
thought to have an unbalanced mind. “ The man
is absolutely crazy,” proclaimed Murat Halsted,
in the early days of the war when Sherman was
manoeuvring in Kentucky ; but shortly after he
was put in command of a division, as General
Grant, whose almost unerring judgment of men
was marvellous, quickly recognised his great
abilities. From thence on he was Grant’s most
trusted and most valued lieutenant. “Sherman
saved Shiloh,” Grant said after the battle, and
then the people began to believe him a great
leader. It was some time before Grant could be
brought into line on Sherman’s projected move-
ment through the enemy’s lines. But at last he
came to agree with the brilliant strategist, and
from that moment never doubted the success of
the march, “ from Atlanta to the sea that
brightest star in Sherman’s crown of fame. The
men who strike out in new paths are not always
cranks—they are the world’s leaders and bene-
factors. People still say kidney disease is not
curable, because that is the old theory. But
there are thousands of men in this and other lands
who write as does Henry M’Namara, of Darling
Harbor, Sydney, N.S.W. “For five years I
was a great sufferer from disease of the kidneys,
•combined with intense pains and a constant heat
across the back and loins. I was under the care
of three doctors, who afforded •me little if any
relief, so that my health gradually declined, and
with an entire' aversion to physical exercise
became a complete invalid. My attention was
drawn to the efficacy of Warner’s safe cure.
With the use of nine bottles my painful symptoms
were entirely removed. Prior to taking it I was
for nearly six months confined to my bed. I have
since taken more of the medicine, and can certify
that it has restored me to health.”

Society
When Bishop Barry was in Sydney he put
his name down for £5O on the contribution-list for
the N.S.W. Women’s College. In response to a
recent application for a cheque, however, he ex-
plained that his promise was only intended to be
conditional on his remaining in Australia, and
that there were so many calls, &0., &o. —in short,
his reverence not only wouldn’t pay, but managed
to make it appear a matter of high moral duty
that he shouldn’t pay. The dear departed Bishop,
on the strength of his promise, got a cheap £5O
worth of kudos, as his openhandedness was widely
advertised.
# * *
Judge Windeyer, of N.S.W. Supreme
Court, has been knighted, and so has his dear
friend, Julian Salomons, Q.C. Judge Windeyer
is admittedly a man of brains and is reckoned,
even by his enemies, the ablest Judge all round
on the N.S.W. bench; but—unless “British
justice ”be dyad —the Mount Rennie trial, if its
facts are officially known in England, should have
blotted out all chance of the head of the State
going out of her way to give to Mr. Windeyer any
special mark of favour. No Judge who had thus
conducted a trial could be knighted in a conn try
blessed by a democratic and independent press. It
is shrewdly suspected that Carington arranged the
knighthood, as also perhaps that of Mr. Salomons,
to whom he was more than effusively civil during
the perky Q.C.’s late visit to England. Anyhow,
Judge Windeyer’s knighthood will eminently suit
the Daringtonian book, as prima-facie it will be a
sort of mild vindication of the grossest act ever
done by any Governor reigning in the name of any
civilised Sovereign claiming to give equal justice
to all men, powerful and obscure alike.

Sara Bernhardt did delight the crowd of
2000 who welcomed her at Spencer-street. How
young, how girlish in her glee ! She sticks to
that little black cr£pe hat, with the yellow and
blue flowers, with a blue travelling-cloak. The
job carriages were astonishingly good—quite
Hopetouny.
# # #
Sara’s handicap is a colossal one—-playing
in French and charging in English.

Immediately after the Jaynil Dan O’Con-
nor’s Hibernian-French speech at the Bernhardt’s
first Sydney reception, the eminent actress with
most of her company took a hasty trip to Glades-
ville Lunatic Asylum. Comment would be cruel.
* # *
Grieving over Christie Murray’s clean-
shaven exit, a Melbourne weekly says; “The
descent of the author of ‘ A Model Father ’ and
‘Joseph’s Coat’ from a walk of literature in
which he excelled to simple and unadulterated
barn-storming is one of the most melancholy
spectacles furnished lately.” To affirm that
Murray excelled as a novelist is little less foolish
than to describe him as a barn-stormer on the
boards. It is equally certain that he possesses no
literary genius, and does display a good deal of
histrionic talent. As an actor he may yet become
more generally admired than his books have been.
Moreover, a man must live. Now The Bulletin
hears that Christie Murray’s literary income had
greatly fallen off for some time before he came out
here on his lecturing expedition. Which accounts
for him returning home in a new capacity, with
the hope of being able to utilise his dead self as a
stepping-stone to higher things, financially
speaking.
* * *
The vacant Melbourne Town-clerkship
brings out a shoal of candidates. Byron Moore
could get it, but has rather grown out of that sort
of thing, although he was terribly hit in the land
boom—they say he met a facer of £30,000.
City Treasurer Thompson ought to have a good
show, and a strong candidate is Miles, South Mel-
bourne’s Town Clerk.
* * #
Col. Tom Price, whose team of Mounted
Rifles appear to have been distinguishing them-
selves at the British military tournaments, has
done a little good in) his generation, by making
the Khakee uniform universal in the Victorian
Militia. The burlesque of the British lobster,
boiled, has gone, also the busby, with its shaving-
brush and bit of red flannel.
#* ■ #
An American actress, arriving in Sydney
lately, drove up to a leading hotel and enquired
for apartments. “We don’t take in single women
here,” was the landlord’s answer. “We can’t all
be married,” returned the bright, little woman as
she turned to go. The landlord, who does not
admit unmarried women presumably because of
their supposed predilection to immorality, should
have more tact than to disclose his reason, more
and kindliness than to insult a woman
‘simply because she had no male protector.

The Tyson, junr. v. Tyson nullity of
marriage case will probably tend to further
emphasise the well-known views of Tyson, senior,
concerning the foolishness of matrimony and the
dangerousness of the fair sex.
Lady Parkes held a semi-regal “recep-
tion ” at Cooma, which was attended by “a large
number of ladies.” Cooma is obviously far ahead
of Sydney in social democracy.

THE EMBEZZLEMENT BOOM.
DEPOSITOR: “Is the Teller in?”
MANAGER: “ No, he has gone away.”
DEPOSITOR: “Ah! gone for a rest, I presume?”
MANAGER (sadly): “N-o; I fancy it’s to avoid arrest! ”
Sister Gertrude, of Molokai fame, has
been married to her Doctor Lutz. Thus ends the
much and gushingly-advertised leper-romance.
The average woman who takes to nursing for
“pure philanthropy” has half-an-eye on the
sick and an eye and a-half on the young doctors.
The fact is that hospital nursing is in itself
hideously-disgusting work —its only really alluring
feature is the way in which it increases a woman’s
matrimonial chances. There now !

Modern Mr. Jackson, at a party : “ Now,
my dear, I want to go home.” Modern Mrs.
Jackson : “ Which home—mine or yours ?”
* * *
The Australian Women’s Christian Tem-
perance Convention has afforded some funny
developments. Lovely woman has very little
humour, and women who are intensely pious have
no humour at all, as piety, for the most part, con-
sists in inability to see a joke. It was resolved
“that the members of the convention write
Christian letters to barmaids,” and the president
of the Tasmanian Association, in writing “to
excuse her absence to-day,” stated that “she
could not come, as the Lord had just blessed her
with a pair of beautiful twins ” [i.c., four infants?).
Just imagine one of the possibilities of woman-
franchise and the near political future. The Pre-
mier (say old Parkes, or the virtuous bachelor
Gillies) rises in his place in Parliament : “I am
sorry to have to say that the Financial Statement,
which was to have been delivered to-night by my
(female) colleague, the honourable the Treasurer,
will have to be postponed for a couple of months,
as the Lord has just blessed her with a pair of
beautiful twins.” We can hear Parkes saying it,
in his pipey treble.
* # *
Judge Hodges fired off an effective petard
last week over what he called the twopenny-half-
penny Masonic case, in Melbourne, filling the
court time six days, merely because there_ was
money in it, to make barristers wag their jaws.
The Judge declared that no tribunal should over-
ride those of the nation—this apropos to a state-
ment that a Masonic Court had already dealt with
the case.
* * *
The race of Victorian Supreme Court
Judges and barristers-at-law appears to be de-
generating. Who ever dreamt of seeing Barry or
Stawell or old Moley weeping over a wrangle with
counsel? Or who would ever think of seeing old
Dick Ireland, Dawson, Aspinall, or Fellowes
crying in court? In the days gone by, when these
able counsel were before the Judges, they were
able to awe the Court. We remember once Judge
Barry mildly hinting to Ireland, Q.C., that he
“had a certain power,” when the Q.C. suddenly
thrust his “specs.” on to his forehead, and ex-
claimed : “ You have, have you ? Well, the sooner
you are deprived of that power the better.” And
in that case the Sheriff was not sent for, and
Barry did not “weep,” and Ireland did not “cry.”
# # #
Mrs. Jackson, of English abduction-noto-
riety, has been interviewed. There were a lot of
causes for her refusal to live with her husband.
He had a bad temper, and (for instance) asked her
when sitting down to dinner immediately after
their marriage, “Where are your aitches?”
Secondly, she married him thinking him indepen-
dent, vhereas he had no money and was so hard-
up that he actually asked her to pay his passage
back from New Zealand. ' Thirdly, she had a
sister and friends and he had a sister and friends.
If they had been friendless orphans they might
have got along very nicely together, as a woman
will forgive anything so long as she has not
another woman beside her to artfully work up her
wrath, which (to generalise boldly once more) is
woman’s pet occupation.

* *
MacPhillibeg came out to Now Zealand,
from the Hielands, as henchman to Sandy Snell,
an old hunks who for years prior to the acquire-
ment of a legacy, his marriage, and its rehabili-
tating influences, had herded with the Maoris
and worn only a gunny-bag as vesture, except on
feast-days, when he donned a potato-sack for
“ best.” Mac. was a second Donald Dinnie
(Donald in his prime was a pairfeck Haircules),
and his prowess as a wrestler and jumper and
weight-thrower fairly staggered the Maoris, no
mean authorities in athletics. They finally came
to the conclusion that he was a magician, and a
chief who had been forcibly induced to take a
back-seat in the arena since Mac.’s advent, at
length offered him two horses for the kilt in which,
he was convinced, lay the secret of Mac.’s invinci-
bility. Mac. quickly took advantage of the offer,
and the Maori as quickly discovered that the sped!
only acted while Mac. was inside the kilt. Mac.
is now one of the richest station-holders in Maori-
land, and the foundations of his fortune were laid
by the horses for which ho swapped away his
national garb.
* * *
British army exams, are to be lowered
to the level of aristocratic intelligence, and
candidates will henceforth require nothing more
than “ a thoroughly well-grounded liberal educa-
tion as English gentlemen ” to pull them
through. This elastic clause is susceptible to any
amount of stretching. Even Lord Hopetoun, who
failed to cram himself to the necessary
pitch of efficiency, might easily have passed for a
soldier on the “gentleman’s education” ticket,
which abolishes the necessity for a lot of absurd
study, and throws Her Majesty’s Army quite open
to the uppah classes again. Everybody knows how
a “swell” like the Duke of Portland is educated,
for instance. Didn’t his Grace observe, in a letter
to the Earl of Durham, that some noble sportsmen
were “ worser ” than the jockeys ? And didn’t the
Prince of Wales write to Lady Mordamjt when
she had the measles : “ You had better lay up for
a few days.”
J « # *
London Savage Club did not come to grief
as our correspondent feared. The members rallied
round like men, and the club is now in a better
position than ever. Sir Somers Vine is now the
hon. secretary, vice Wade, and he has already,
backed by an energetic committee, made a won-
derful change in the place.
* * *
A man was picked up at Bondi (Sydney)
on Queen’s Birthday with a scalp wound, and was
taken to Prince Alfred Hospital, where he was
identified by a woman as her husband. Shortly
afterwards the authorities were informed that she
had made a mistake, and had found her rightful
partner, who had also fallen out of a tramcar.
How many women, we wonder, would be ready to
identify their husbands by the fact of their having
fallen out of trains on— hie ! —Queensh Birthday’;
* * *
Mrs. Grundy, in Melbourne, is screaming
for the instant execution of a certain woman,
without reflecting that it is a woman purely of
her own creation. This woman has a prodigious
advertisement of two convictions, and her business
is to render unobtrusive those healthy young
citizens who do not approach this world according
to the laws of Grundy.
# # #
Someone writes to say that one of the lady-
teachers in a big Sydney public-school allows her
best-behaved pupils, as a reward for good conduct,
to polish the said teacher’s boots. Another
suburban lady-teacher “goes one better” by per-
mitting the “best girl in the class” to rub her
corns !

* * *
The fussy parsons have a new grievance in
Melbourne —those poor newsboys and newsgirls !
Now, let the most experienced police-reporter
run the drag-net over his memory, and see if he is
able to fish up anything like a list of newsboys or
newsgirls coming to crime—and then ask the re-
porter of nine days’experience if the clergyman’s
son has been up yet ? Ye fools and blind !
# * #
Reseigh Martin writes in a Sydney Sunday
paper that a beautiful superstition of his child-
hood —that no Jew ever went to gaol—has been
dissipated by his residence in Darlinghurst prison.
In January last, he states, the Rabbi had a
congregation of 28 within the walls. He says
that no offender is ever without the pale of the
active sympathies of the Synagogue, which is
mightier than all the churches put together ; that
the immured Hebrews enjoy two Sundays in every
week and a number of holy days ; that, whilst
R.C. and particular Anglican prisoners are com-
pelled to eat flesh food all the year round, the
J ews indulge in potted fi sh, &c., on every one of their
numerous festivals ; that they are allowed private
interviews with their woraenkind ; and are per-
mitted, on the Asiatic religious plea, to retain
their beards provided the same are intact at the
time of admisssion ; that their distinct preference
is towards bigamy and valueless cheques ; and
that they are generally to be found at work in the
gaol tailors’ shop.
* * *
A London paper says that at a recent party
at Mrs. Campbell-Praed’s “an Australian was
present who has a thousand pounds a day to
spend.” Yes, perhaps he has for a few days or
weeks at a stretch, but there is no Australian
living who has a thousand pounds a day to spend,
though we ourselves have occasionally lived (for a
few seconds) at the rate of £lOOO a day. And if
there does happen to be extant so wealthy an Aus-
tralian he may be depended upon to “ fist ” it very
carefully instead of throwing it about. The one
great lesson which every wealthy Australian learns
in England is that it is “good form” to be
mean. Lo:>k at that story about the mutton-
millionaire and the Marchioness of Salisbury.

Rev. Mark Guy Fflarse is, or was to un-
cork the XXX of Goshen to a half-crown
audience at Melbourne Town Hall, it being repre-
sented that the Evangelical upper crust has been
unable to hear him, because they cannot endure
being elbowed by the vulgar Evangelical crowd. For,
mind you, the Billocrats never mix with the Tillo-
crats, and in every chapel the strata of caste is
defined with Hindoo precision.
Ballarat Courier Clark gave such of the
children who sang at the Victorian Industrial
Exhibition a brand-new threepenny-piece. In
return they gave him “ three ringing cheers,” At
a penny apiece, replete with the ring of sound
metal.

There lives a rich squatter near Hay,
Who married a widow, they say ;
But lately he reckoned
On getting a second,
By putting the widdy away.
ADDRESS TO THE DUCHESS.
Interesting kid,
With your little face
Usually h d
In a cloud of lace ;
Ere your mottled hand
Feeding-bottle clutches,
Ere you understand
You’ve a future grand,
Site who rules the land
Dubbetli you a duchess.
Such is life,
Duchess Fife !
Babes of women born
Ashes are and dust.
Lesson laugh’d to scorn
By the uppah crust ;
Nature finished you
With artistic touches,
And your blood is blue.
O’er your cradle new
Cry a noble crew—
“ Here we have a duchess.”
ffufli is life,
Duchess Fife !
Many thousand brats
Pine away and die,
As on filthy mats
They are left to lie;
Mother on the booze,
Father still on crutches,
Ne’er a hope to lose
Have those infants, whose
Feet would fit the shoes
Of Her Grace the Duchess.
Such is life.
Duchess Fife!

“The evil that men do lives after them.”
The late brewer Montgomerie, of Melbourne,
a year or two ago smuggled a diamond neck-
lace over the border from Sydney, was detected,
and had to pay a thumping cheque in duty to tne
Victorian Government. The facts were widely
advertised in the papers at the time, and now two
masked burglars turn up at 3 a.m. at Mrs. Mont-
gomerie’s residence with: “We want your diamond
necklace.” “Itis at the bank,” says the widow.
And so it was.* Thus she luckily got rid of them
for a paltry £5.
Two Forbes (N.S.W.) constables, who had
a “ clue” to some cheques stolen with the Trundal
mail-bags, were busy raking the bottom of a
lagoon when, sure enough, they fished out two
cheques. Surprise 1 One cheque was for £lOO in
favour of the senior constable, and the other tor
£5O in favour of the ordinary peeler, and to each
“ for his energy in raking out the lagoon. 1p9 1d ,:
The cheques were made payable at the (mytnica )
“ City Bank, Carcoar.” The energetic officers say
they want another “ clue ” badly.

320 men, all young and stalwart and hard-up,
answered an advertisement the other day tor a
barman’s vacancy ; 23 applicants for a coachman a
billet presented themselves to an advertiser living
at Brighton, 15 miles from Melbourne before 7
o’clock in the morning on which the aclvt.
appeared in a Melbourne paper ; a boarding-house
keeper who advertised for an errand-boy received
a dozen applications from broken-down gentle-
men offering to do i 4 anything about the house for
board and lodging and whatever you choose to
give besides.”

This is how the medical board polished off
the case of the recently-hanged Johnston, of
Ballarat :
We, the medical practitioners appointed by the Chief
Secretary to inquire into the insanity of James Johnston, a
prisoner under sentence of death in the Ballarat gaoh do
hereby certify that we have inquired into such insanity,
and find that the said James Johnston is not insane.'
There may be a lot of Latin and Greek roots in

JUVENILE NURSE: “Yes, sir.”
PARSON: “Is it a boy or a girl?”
JUVENILE NURSE: “ Please, sir, we don’t know till it’s christened.”

couldn’t “incmire into the insanity ” of a man who

Lady Jersey to the Boys’ Brigade: “My
dear boys, there is nothing in this country that
does not lie in your power. The one thing
required for success in life is to know what you
want and never rest until you have got it. io
this Young Australia replies that the Governor-
ship of his own country, which under present cir-
cumstances is outside the scope of his aspirations,
is one of the things he wants and that he will
never be happy till he gets it.

Lord Kintore thus profoundly expresses
his conclusions at the result of his expensive
Transcontinental trip : “No doubt much of the
land is good and much is bad.” The S.A. Go-
vernment will assuredly rise up and thank Kintore
(140 times for this remarkable and well-considered
opinion.

He appeared at Adelaide railway-station in a
greasy wide-awake cap, dirty coat and pai ~
was unshaven, notwithstanding the fa ,
had travelled hundreds of miles by rad, where a

raised a feeble cheer, but the thousands on the

Mr. Solomon Blay, aged 80, the old-
established family hangman of Hobart (Tas.), is
reported to have resigned “owing to old age ana
the general decline of his mental faculties. W e
used to imagine that a hangman could get along
without faculties, but possibly we were wrong—at
all events Mr. Blay is suffering from a species of
decadence in his think-tank, and is giving up
business in consequence. As a result of this un-
foreseen circumstance tenders will presently be
called for the purchase of an old and prosperous
hanging connection, doing a steady business and
capable of indefinite extension, together with
plant, fixtures, goodwill, and asphyxiating imple-
merits. The retiring proprietor will give every
assistance and encouragement to the new-comer,
and he pledges himself not to hang or otherwise
interfere with the business anywhere within the
province. It may be mentioned that during Mr.
Blay’s tenure of the business upwards of 200 cus-
tomers, have called upon him,, and they have all
been sent away—through a hole in the flooring
perfectly satisfied. The plant includes a com-
modious rope with a spacious air-hole at the end, a
large vacancy in the air to hold clients, a valuable
jerk which prevents the client g< ing away before
the transaction is completed, and a dull, sicken-
ing thud which has been favourably commented
on by the press on many occasions.

N.S.W. public-school English, Teacher
asks pupils to construct sentences showing use of
word “ mortal.” Sample of result ; “My mother
was mortal wild with me this morning !”
Recent years have developed such wonder-
ful discoveries in the fields of science, art, and
manufactures, that it seems almost strange that
nature is not completely superseded and every-
thing performed artificially. Where our fore-
fathers used the flint and steel we employ the
wax match, and instead of the old fashioned
lamps and candles we have gas and electric
lights. We have also replaced the old rumbling
stage coach with our railways and electric
trams. Generally speaking, so much progress
has not been made in the field of medicine,
very few really important discoveries (with one
notable exception) having been made ; disease
still treads its insidious course among the in-
habitants of this fair land. What is it, and
how does it get into the body ? Disease makes
its attack so gently and develops itself so
gradually, that many people never suspect its
deadly presence until a dangerous stage is
reached. They neglect the first signs, as a feel-
ing of weakness, dull eyes, fickle appetite,
pains in the sides, dark or muddy urine, con-
tinual tired feeling and restless and wakeful
nights, these symptoms only too plainly tell
the approach of some physical affliction ; bitterly
do people thus attacked rue their carelessness
and thoughtlessness in disregarding the early
and curable stages. A timely use of Clements
Tonic would have restored the health and vigour,
insured rest and rebuilt the physical and
nervous constitutions, and reinvigorated the
whole physical structure ; its price is so low
that a long course of it can be taken for less
money than one visit to a doctor will cost and
vou have a medicine which has proved its
virtues in hundreds of cases, whereas the
doctor's prescription is hap-hazard and experi-
mental. A gentleman who objects to have his
name published, writes; “ Castlereagh-street,
Sydney,—Dear Sir, —For some time past I have
been in the hands of the doctors, and can truth-
fully assert that 1 obtained not the slightest
benefit. I heard of Clements I’onic, and have
taken three bottles with such marked good
results as to cause me to write and congratulate
you on the very excellent virtues of your
remedy. It has completely cured me of all my
disorders, and I feel myself an entirely different
man. A friend of mine to whom I gave a
bottle, half of which he has taken, says he feels
wonderfully better in his general health, and,
with myself, begs to cordially thank you for
giving the public the benefit of your skill in
manufacturing such a splendid tonic. Ido not
wish my name used in print, but hope you are
very prosperous in the sale of your medicine, as
you justly deserve.” We must, however, give
one word of warning. Some unscrupulous
traders are only too anxious to force some vile
imitation of Clements Tonic upon an unsuspect-
ing purchaser. We consider the man who
practices such deceit and will trifle with the
sufferings of unfortunate men and women, to be
destitute of all honor and honesty, or as
Shakespeare puts it —
A stony adversary, an inhuman wretch,
Incapable of pity, void and empty,
From every drachm of mercy.
Reader, take this lesson to heart, and read
those words to the swindler who tries to impose
upon you with a substitute, which he knows is
worthless, ask for and insist on Clements Tonic.
Laboratories, Newtown, N.S.W.
THE CHINESE QUESTION.
Ah Sin was held by people vers d
In all his Chinkee ways accurs’d
To be a thing polluted ;
And such as of his habits knew
Asreed in thinking he vyas to
This sunny land unsuited.
For, like the British lords and earls,
He set a snare for little girls
(With lollypops he baited);
And maidens, having tried a smoke,
Were apt to wish, when they awoke,
They’d never been created.
And when The Bulletin began
To drop upon the Chinaman,
So “patient” and so “civil,”
The dailies said he had to go
(As all who read the papers know),
Ah Sin was such a devil.
They scorned the very thought indeed
Of letting here the lepers breed
As thick as coals in coal-sacks ;
The Governments could not allow
Invasions by the cunning Chow,
On whom they put a poll-tax.
Ah Sin lay quiet for awhile;
We overlooked the heathen vile,
Who still among us tarried,
Until he called, the other day,
Upon a Government to say
He wanted to get married.
“ Him live a welly ploper life,_
Him go and fetch a ploper wile
And daughters—most pertikler ;
No Double when he come again ?”
(For morals, as was very p’ain,
This stranger was a stickler.)
The Law it scratched its addled head,
The “ Act ” was taken out and road -
’Twas nice for Sin to marry ;
But O, alas ! this was the thing—
No vessel had a right to bring
More Chows than it should carry !
'Twas hard he could not take the trip.
And then return, on any ship,
Aglow with wedded blisses- —
Accompanied by daughters nine,
As well as that old gal divine
Alleged to be his “ missis.”
The Law it shook its skinny hand
And begged of Sin to understand
With grief ’twould like to bellow ;
Then mentioned, in an undertone,
“Just let the ladies come alone,
Or, say in pairs, old fellow.”
Ah Sin he wears a smiling face.
He’ll soon have kindred at his place,
And annual babies chubby ;
And as his daughters (Jubilate !)
Shall each be big enough to mate,
Why each will get a hubby.
Right soon the Chows will swarm around,
On every street they will abound
As thick as coals in coal-sacks ;
’Twas bad enough to keep Ah Sin,
But when we let his women in
What use will be the Poll Tax?

The Shares in the above Company having been
applied for, the following Prospectus and Report
is published for private information only.
The Capital of the Company is £30,000 divided
into 00,000 Shares of 10s. each—l7,soo of the said
Shares are fully paid up to 10s. each. 35,000 of
the said Shares paid up to 9s. each, and contribut-
ing to the extent of 10s. per Share, balance Is. per
Share, payable at calls not exceeding 3d. per Share
per month, and 7500 contributing Shares to be held
in reserve.
The objects for which the Company is estab-
lished are as follows :
To purchase, acquire and work certain leasehold
lands comprised and held under mineral leases
25, 44, 45, making a total area of 1G acres, situated
in the country of Buller, Parish of Clarence, in
the colony of New South Wales.
Together with such other deeds and stipulations
as are contained in the articles of association of
the said Company registered in the office of
the Supreme Court of Queensland as a “No
Liability Company,” under the Company’s Act,
1863, the IGth day of May, 1891. Registered
Office of the Company, New Zealand Chambers,
Brisbane. R. Falkiner, Legal Manager,
Report of MR. H. TRENEAR (late Assayer
and Smelter at the Mount Morgan Company’s
Works), on the “ Rise and Shine ” Mine, Tooloom,
and immediately adjoining leases
Brisbane, February 17, 1891.
Having made a careful examination of the mine
called “J. Payne’s Gold Mine” (known as the
“Rise and Shine,” and owned by Payne and
Sons), I have much pleasure in handing you the
following notes on what I saw there, for your
consideration.
The Property is situated at Tooloom, Parish of
Clarence, County of Buller, N.S.W., one of the
earliest and richest Alluvial Border Fields ; 30
miles from Kiilarney Railway Station (Q.), about
30 miles from Fairfield, and consists of Gold
Leases No. 25, 44 and 45, area 1G acres.
The Lode runs about 10° S. of W. and N. of E.,
and underlays North 40° from the perpendicular.
In width it varies from two feet to a few inches,
but it will, I consider, average about a foot of
stone consisting of quartz, clay, felspar, red oxide
of iron, and decomposed rock associated with a
little arsenical iron pyrites and black clay-slate in
bands.
The gold occurs chiefly, as coarse flakey gold, in
nodules of quartz, iron oxide, : nd pink coloured
felspar on the hanging wall, some of this stone,
when broken, actually hanging together with the
precious metal.
I think I may safely say that payable gold js
found throughout the reef, but in places it is
abundant and at first sight would seem to indicate
a patchy character, but from what I saw I con-
sider that all the lode will have to bo treated.
The footwall is porphyry, hard and smooth and
regular ; the hanging-wall is serpentine, easy for
boring, and yet sufficiently compact to stand
without timber. About two-thirds of a green-
hide bag—weighing probably 70lbs. —of stone
broken from east end of bottom of shaft, lode one
foot thick, and dealt with in my presence, gave
loz. lOdwts. 22grs. of clean bright gold, and
another bag broken from the western end (also in
my presence, and two days later after a shot or
two had been fired) where the reef was from four
to six inches thick, a dish of which gave such a
quantity of beautiful coarse gold that we did not
consider it necessary to pan the lot.
Wo entered the mine by a vertical shaft 45ft.
deep. This shaft cuts the lode which has been
sunk on the underlay for a distance of about 140 ft.,
where the samples spoken of above .were broken.
From the bottota of the shaft (45ft.) to the south
(up-hill) the underlay shaft continues through to
grass, a distance of some 100 ft. ; but this part of
the workings is in bad condition, and it was
hardly safe to attempt to examine them. No
stoping except within a few feet of the surface
has been done, so that almost the whole of the
reef is available for breaking down at once, or so
fast as the present system of working will permit.
I may here mention that there is no battery or
machine on the field, and that the gold is ob-
tained by Payne and his sons washing the stone
and picking and dollying the specimens; by this
means they have Mint Account Sales for over
£l5OO for gold sold.
I estimate there are from 60 to 70 tons of stone
in the paddock, which, the owners say, will go
from soz. to Goz. per ton—some of it I could see
is very good, but probably 2oz. to the ton would
be a fair value.
Both of these, so essential to cheap and success-
ful mining are abundant, and close at hand. The
Tooloom River, with a never-failing supply, is
within 400 or 500 yards of the mine, and the ridges
are covered with the finest timber.
A dray-road from Kiilarney to the township
and neighbourhood runs within half a mile or so
of the property, and a small outlay would make a
wheel-track right into the mine.
The lode is not large but it is rich enough, at
the deepest point touched, to satisfy the most
exacting, neither could I notice any indication in
the strata or general surroundings that would lead
one to look for a speedy change, or anything that
would unfavourably affect the continuity or pro-
ductiveness of the lode. It (the lode) is rapidly
getting into deep ground, and the present bottom
of the underlay shaft must have a cover of 130 to
150 ft., perpendicular.
The surface of the country is broken and moun-
tainous-characteristic of England district—
but it is admirably suited for mining by tunnels,
and, in the event of anything being done at the
“ Rise and Shine,” a tunnel is the first considera-
tion for speedy and economical development.
After spending three days at the Mine, and
carefully examining the lode and its contents, I
have come to the conclusion that the property is a
valuable one and well worth the attention of
mining-men. It is not a big reef, but it is rich in
Prospectus.
gold, and when the present primitive, laborious,
and costly mode of working gives place to a better
gvstem of mining, and care and skill are exercised
in the reduction of the quartz, the Mine ought to
w l,andSOm ‘ 1) '- H. THEN E AR.
Addendum to my Report on the “ Rise and Shine
Mine, Tooloom, N.S. IV., dated hth February,

Payne’s mine—that a six-acre lease hardly afforded

ranted by the prospects of the reef, I measured
and pegged out on your behalf, two ten-acre
leases, one on each end of and adjoining Payne s
ground, thus securing a length of over 30 chains
on the line of reef with a width on the North
underlay, sufficient to protect the lode to a

MOST EXTRAORDINARY STATE-
. MENT.
A MODERN MIRACLE.
To the Editor of the Wellington (Salop)
Journal.
Dear Sir, —In your paper of October 20 I saw
the account of what 1 considered a most extra-
ordinary statement, wherein “ Mrs. Mary Ann
Foster, of 48, Greenfield-terrace, Gateshead, had
been cured of rheumatism in the limbs in 15
minutes by using St. Jacobs oil. - ’ Now, this
statement of itself seems incredible, but when the
article stated, further, that “for a long time her
legs had been so stiff that she could not sit up in
bed/’that “the ligamen's of her joints seem to
have grown together,” and that “ her case was
considered hopeless ” it seemed a miracle which 1
determined to investigate. I accordingly called
on Mrs. Foster for the purpose of learning the
truth. Judge of my surprise when that lady told
mo that every word was true. She said for
months previous to using the oil she had been
confined to her bed suffering the most excruciating
agony night and day, but that in 15 minutes after
the application she experienced relief from pain,
and in less thnn ci week she was up zincl about the
house —a well woman. Mrs. Foster says this n as
more than 12 months ago and she has not had
any return of the .disease. She is as well and
sti’ong as she ever was in her life, and (naturally)
recommends St. Jacobs oil to everybody—in fact,
is a living advertisement for St. Jacobs oil. 1
hand you these facts solely for the benefit of the
public, believing that a remedial agent possessing
such wonderful power to conquer pain and relieve
suffering should be made known everywhere. 1
am glad to see that your journal, as well as the
press generally, is taking up the matter, winch is
quite right.
I remain, yours respectfully,
THOMAS HUTCHKISS.
4G, Napier-street, Newcastle-on-Tyne.

BUCKINGHAM PARK ESTATE,
IVIEDLOW (Blue Mountains).
This charming Estate has its Railway Platform almost
adjoining, and its level land proves it to be eminently
adapted for residential purposes. Nature seems to have
lavished all her varied charms on BUCKINGHAM PARK,
which is, without exception, the CREAM or PICKED
SPOT on the BLUE MOUNTAINS.
BUCKINGHAM PARK ESTATE
Is beautifully surveyed. The Allotments are unusually
large—from 100 up to 180 feet frontages, with long depths
—thus enabling buyers to have Garden, Lawns,
etc., if they so desire. In fact, it may be truly said, there
is no finer Residential Land on the Mountains.
The BELGRAVIA HOTEL, MEDLOW, which cost £4OOO,
is now open for visitors, and is the First Hotel on the
Mountains supplied with the Electric Light, and the
Proprietor is willing to supply any cottages built on the
estate with the electric light. Two elegant Villas are
now nearly finished on this estate. The rapid advance in
the price of land at BLACKHEATH during the past three
years, amounting to fully 300 per cent., clearly shows that
early buyers at Buckingham Park will make a splendid
investment. Title is TORRENS, and the terms are liberal
and to suit buyers. For particulars, apply to the Pro-
pnetor,
WILLIAM EYRE,
113 King Street, SYDNEY.

Industrial Queensland.
Mb. R. E. Jaeman, whose portrait we give with
this issue, is undoubtedly the premier manufac-
turing saddler of Queensland. He not only
manufactures for the trade, but has extensive
re ail establishments in Brisbane, Townsville, and
Beaudesert (Q.). His factory is situated in Ade-
laide-street, Brisbane, almost at the rear of
his retail store in Queen-stieet. In the
manufacturing-room some novel labour-saving
machinery is in use, noticeably the sewing-
machine for binding together traces and other
heavy pieces of leather. All the motive power in
use is worked by electricity, which is found by
Mr. Jarman the most suitable for his purposes.
A glance over the workshop showed a multitude
of men and boys employed at various tasks. When
but 18 years of age (and still in his apprentice-
ship) Mr. Jarman was selected to manage a busi-
ness in Oooma, there his career as a successful
saddler may be said to have commenced. He
exhibited saddlery in Sydney in 1875, beating all
competitors. Since that time he has made Exhi-
bition goods a special feature of his trade, and can
now show 15 medals, a silver cup, and a large
number of diplomse, amounting in all to over 100
prizes. He exhibited with great success in Sydney
in 1879, Brisbane 1878, and also at Bombala,
Mackay, Maryborough, Townsville, Roma, Mel-
bourne, and at • the London, Indian, and
Colonial Exhibition. The saddle exhibited
MR. R. E. JARMAN.
in the atter instance (made by Mr. Jarman
personally) was presented to, and accepted
by H.R.H. the Prince of Wales. This saddle is
spoken of as a remarkable piece of workmanship,
and it stamped the maker as a master of his art.
It may be mentioned that Mr. Jarman was for
some time in business in Wagga, Narandera and
Hay in conjunction with Mr. Robert Cassin, of
Wagga, but was, in his own words, “driven out
of the Murrutnbidgee country by the great
drought of 1878. ” He then settled in Queensland,
and notwithstanding his great perseverance and
energy, has suffered considerably from the wave
of commercial depression which lias swept over
Queensland for some time back. Nevertheless, he
has a great notion of Queensland as the premier
colony of Australia, and fully intends to proceed
with many improvements in connection with his
business. Mr. Jarman is a man of great industrial
resource, the very man for a young colony. In
addition to high-class goods for private use Mr.
Jarman caters largely for country and station use,
and his goods are admitted, for style and durability,
to be unequalled not only in Australia but through-
out the world.
Round the Shops and Shows.*
It is quite wonderful how a woman’s body
can seemingly be elongated or shortened according
to the fashion of the day. This feat is accom-
plished by altering the make of the corsets. For
instance, now that long bodies are in vogue, the
corset is made some inches longer than those of
last year; in fact, they are made to reach over the
hips. Messrs. Cecil Hordern and Co., of 486, 488,
and 490 George-street, have a splendid variety of
corsets in all the best makes, Thompson’s “ True-
fit ” being perhaps the most suitable for the pre-
sent fashion. The “Pearl ” ranks next in merit,
but as Messrs. Hordern and Co. have such a
splendid stock on hand, ladies cannot do better
than go and see for themselves.
* * *
Ceylon tea is certainly the purest in the
market at present. Messrs. Harris and Co., 18
Royal Arcade, sell an excellent tea at 2s. per lb.
In households where this tea is used headaches
are unknown—just try it.

Lord Byron never liked to see a woman
eat, but, then, Byron was altogether too affectedly
sensitive. At all events ladies of the present day
should be thankful that all men are not so
squeamish, otherwise Mr. Tollemache would have
opened the handsome ladies room at the City
Buffet in vain. Certainly it is crowded every day
with ladies and their cavaliers who have not been
slow in finding out where they can get the best
dinner in town from one shilling upward.

W. MOLE.
The Old Refrain.—A Song.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
He stands upon the crowded deck,
She weeps upon the quay,
The good ship slowly swings her bows
Towards the darkening sea.
And as the cheers arise from those
Who ne'er may meet again,
Above them all, he seems to hear
Her sing the old refrain—
Absence makes the heart grow fonder
Though the parting gives us pain,
Time shall prove our love the stronger
When we meet again.
She kneels before the altar-rails
A proud and happy bride ;
He lies beneath the Southern Gross,
Her letter by his side.
The noisy creek that ripples past
The home he toiled to gain,
No more will mingle with the voice
That sang the glad refrain
Absence makes the heart grow fonder
Though the parting gives us pain,
Time shall prove our love the stronger
When me meet again.
H. H. CRIPPS CLARK.
For Her Sake.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Be careful, girl, I see the evil glitter
Gleam from his sensuous eye when you are here,
And oftentimes the wanton words and bitter
Drop from his lips when you are passing near ;
His voice and smile to thee are very pleasures,
Untutored in the world as thou hast been ;
What are to thee thy best and dearest treasures
Are counted by him nothing, girl, I ween.
I do not know thee, but thy face is kindly—
Like someone whom I knew in days “lang syne,"
And for her sake I would not let thee blindly
Be thus deceived, without a word of mine.
You know me not, 1 am to thee a stranger,
But stranger, still I am to thee a friend ;
I see where thou cannot thy very danger,
X see, what thou will not, what is the end.
I speak the word for her of whom I’ve spoken,
And tor thy sake to whom I speak to now,
That nought can mend the bruised reed and broken,
Nor life reclaim the dry and withered bough ;
Then, girl, retain the lustre of thy jewel,
And spurn his burning glances as thou ought;
Remember that his wanton face and cruel
Are worthy not one moment of thy thought.
Good-bye ; and ere good bye, am I forgiven
For having seen with plainer eyes than thine ?
’Twas for thy welfare, girl, that I have striven,
And for “ her sake” I knew in days “lang syne,”
We met but once, our meeting and our parting,
I saw one other tho’ when thou wert nigh,
And hard it was to quell the tears from starting,
And sadder memories smother. Girl! Good-bye.
Dubbo.
