[?]
BIG Maequarie-street North, Sydney, June 3, 1891.
Mv Dear Moorabinda,—
The second phase of La Bernhardt beside the beautiful
harbour commenced with a reception dejeuner at her
hotel, followed by the mayoral reception in the Town
hall, when, “owing to a bungle in the issuing of the
invitation-cards ” (any occult reader can hear me winking
violently just here) one handful turned up when two
handfuls were expected. “La Marseillaise,” played by-
Mr. Sykes on the organ, and comprehensive food
arranged on tables prettily decked out with pink and
white camellias, were the trimmings of the reception,
which commenced at the wide-awake hour of 2.30 p.m.
to give Sara B. a chance to catch the Southern train.
The reception was just the same as any other crush of
like description. The enthusiasm of the French welcome,
however, was missing from the civic ceremonies, where
most of the women only glared hauntingly at the pifecede
resistance, and one cadaverous young lady of forty or so
sniffed scornfully to her companion, “ I don’t call her
beautiful; why, I could look as nice if I was painted an
inch deep all over like that.”
Then Sara B.’s wild and frantic attire made some of the
fretwork-frocked local divinities exchange “did you ever”
glances. She wore a gown of ivory silk, covered with
splashes of ink-blue, and otherwise glorified with dainty
lace; a Manola cloak in dark-blue, with high collar, and
a wide hat of gilt filagree and black gauze, trimmed with
black ostrich feathers ; over all, a long veil—almost to her
knees -of black spotted tulle. Beside this butterfly
fanciful figure, you may guess the mousey-coloured tweeds
and limp fawn hair of local loveliness looked more hope-
less than ever—nevertheless, some women managed to
stand out. The Mayoress wore her first-reception toilet
of lavender-grey, bordered in cream and flesh-pink flowers.
The festivities were over by half-past four o’clock.
Apropos— (everything is apropos of the Bernhardt just
now) a certain section of S} r dney alleged crime de la
crime— the very top skimming of the social dish, are
going to be extremely exclusive over the great tra-
gedienne, who, it seems, “ is not proper for US to know.’’
Several of them were invited to meet her on a recent
occasion, but the cards were snappishly declined, and some
person on asking a daughter of these socialqaokers if her
parent intended attending, was met by the ’orty rejoinder
“Do you think my mother would, go .to meet HER?”
puling the stress on exactly 7 as above. And this is very
interesting coming as it does from a set of women most
of whom have a manacled skeleton behind the arras—the
“handled” mother of the lady quoted having descended
directly from a beetle-browed gentleman--not a harmless
rabbit-poacher by any means—who left his country for
ills country's good.
It is indeed odd that any “society ” person should hide
her countenance from the first actress of the day after
such lavish recent patronage cf a fourth-rate article who
wasn’t even conspicuously exemplary, or pock-marked, or
suffering from any other physical disability that goes to
make a woman’s woman.
I was one of the few who rushed to the barriers, at
Redfern, to see the last, in a temporary sense, of Madame
Bernhardt, who, with one or two members of her com-
pany, left by “special” shortly after the express—the
rest of the troupe going by the earlier train. For the
journey “she” wore a blue, long cloak edged with blue
chenille over a warm gown of, tartan velvet,’ and was glee-
ful in her idiomatic praises of the arrangements the
station-master at Redfern had made for her Comfort ; but
the rest of the company looked dreadfully unhappy. By
the way, the men of the B. troupe are' mostly blase
middle-aged heroes over whom the throbbing hearts of
Toorak and Potts Point will not absolutely break—not in
the daylight, anyhow. Some of them are short and
hoarse and puffy, filling out their garments as a German-
sausage fills its bladder; the leading man-the Soarpia,
Armand, &c., of the party-is certainly rather an
advance on the rest, but even he wore shepherds’-plaid
trousers and looked homesick. The lady coadjutors are
more possible, and I don’t think their excessive lack of
English will interfere with their success amongst the
Australian gilded youth—one tall and radiant nymph with
henna-dyed hair and a big emerald ring upon her finger
being sure to do “Gaiety” business among the hupper
suckles.
The Jersey lev6e, on 26th ult., went off as usual, and if
one judges pedestrianism as a sign of hollow pockets, it
must be a sad truth that the possession of dress-clothes
doesn’t necessarily imply that they hold a cab-fare. To
see bevies of men perambulating up Bridge-street in
full war-paint at 2.30 p.m. is a very odd spectacle, and on
;he occasion of the last levCe I never before saw so many
“ gentlemen attending ” doing it on their own soles. Of
course there is no disgrace if a man walks from here to
Tophet if he likes, but when he is in a blazing shirt-front-
and assumes quite an Imperial air—then it is certainly
time for him to call a hansom. Some of them didn’t
seem to mind a bit, though, and after “ it” was all over I
met one well-known ex-legal magnate proudly doing
the block with his top-coat wide open over his laundried
buzzum.
The first trial of Band Wednesday in Sydney Botanic
Gardens was made on 27th ult., when the attendance was
microscopic and surprised-looking, and the devotees who
rioted on little camp-stools at threepence a time seemed
harassed by the fact that the grass wasn’t quite dr} 7
enough to be hygienic. Lady J. (with two of the little
Jerseys in tow) was present on the lawn awhile, but even
her magic presence couldn’t redeem the situation, which
fell hopelessly flat—the Bernhardt reception absorbing
most of the people who would have been there otherwise.
By the way, if Band Wednesdays are going to be
"fashionable,” thb band ought to see that its repertoire is
“ sich,” The music on the first occasion was simply tem-
per-trying.
Twenty-three Sydney bachelors paid off their scores (in
an anticipatory way, considering that the season is only
just shooting), by inviting a crowd of Sydney hostesses
and their daughters to a ball at “ Nithsdaje ” on 26th ult.
The rooms were gay with draperies of strawberry and
buttercup-coloured art muslin, palms, evergreens, etc.,
and the programmes were white and gold—the supper-
table groaned, and the novel “ oyster-room ” was simply
rushed. Vice-regality was there, attended by every
member of the “sweet,” Lad3' Jersey wearing geranium-
red duchesse, contrasted with white and gold embroidery,
diamond ornaments, and the rest of the dressing was
smart. White led, with pink and “yaller” as second
favourites, and it was remarked that never had local
belledom looked so charming as it did on this occasion,
under the subdued light of the yellow lamp-shades. But
what was the tun of looking lovely when you were in a
hopeless majority ? There were eighty-eight ladies and
only fifty-five dress-suits—“one woman and three-fifths
of one to every man,” as one of the many wall-flowers
mathematically put it. Consequently, nobody had the
chance to dance the soles off her satin slippers.
Referring to balls, it is said that the reason Lady J.
didn’t actively patronise a recent big hop was that she
understood that Lady X. had been sent a card and in-
tended attending, and some people are again saying that
Lady J. is too hostile altogether. But is she ? It is very
christianUke to say that Lady J. ought to be on calmer
terms with our ex-impropriety, but yet one cannot see
where she is to get her precedent from. Lady J. is the
chosen representative of a straight-laced old lady who
won’t allow a woman to enter her presence with whom
the breath of scandal has had anything to do, so it is hardly
likely that Lady Jersey is going to encourage people of
the X. brand to run tame about Government House ! More
especially as the fickle populace might an}' day relegate
her and her champion into the very utmost social
regions.
On 23th ult. Lady Jersey’s second daughter, Mary, was
fortunate enough to have a birthday, to signalise which a
children’s dance was held at Government House. Like
all children’s affairs it passed off most successfully, the
little hostess in cream surah and the small guests in their
gay-coloured party frocks enjoying every minute as only
children can. The cotillon, in which each child received
a gift, was a great success. Misses Innes, Lamb, and
Salomons were the “grown-ups” invited to amuse the
smaller guests.
At a time when you are desperately weary of the one
eternal argument always advanced in favour of Woman’s
Suffrage - that Parliament would be the synonym of
political purity under the immaculate influence of the
female vote-it is cynically interesting to read of the
“ unseemly conduct ” of a couple of women nominated as
probable members of the Women’s College Council
(elected on 30th ult.) who “ so far forgot their dignity and
that of the position they are seeking to attain as to con-
duct‘a personal canvass, and to secure from well-meaning
but foolish persons their proxies, then using them to
plump for themselves.”
Cowen’s “Rose Maiden” cantata, performed at the
Parramatta Town Hall, by the members of St. John’s
choir, assisted by Mrs. Massey, Miss -Sheridan Reed, Frank
Brewer (suffering from relaxed throat), Herr Pechotsch,
&c., was more an ornamental than a musical success—the
floral decorations by the choir-girls being really the most
artistic aspect of the show. The soprano (Mrs. Massey)
was in excellent voice, hut the orchestration was too
heavy for the soloists, and the general effect was like the
cry of a cricket in a quartz-crushing machine. Neverthe-
less the audience (a crowd) meted out indiscriminate and
rapturous applause.
1 have received from Madame Kellermann a copy of her
latest composition, a “ Tarantelle” in P. minor,dedicated
to Mrs. Sydney Burdekin. Nobody but a proficient
should attempt the “Tarantelle,” which has several diffi-
cult passages and must he pla} - ed at top speed with only
one diminuendo to get your instrumental breath on ; but
for those who can “clown on the ivories” it is a vivacious
airy composition sure to meet with a hearty reception
everywhere.
One of the most successful private single-handed affairs
(most of the hops now are on the trades-union plan) held
in Sydney this season was the “ Karooia” dance the other
Friday evening, when Mrs. Alfred Cape entertained her
friends in a most enjoyable manner. The hoste'ss wore a
Parisian-looking gown of electric-grey brocade contrasted
with pink and satin, and her troupe of guests aired then-
purple and fine linen. A special interest was lent the
rooms 1)\- the presence of one debutante —Miss Darley.
It may interest Wesleyans to know—in connection with
the recent camp-meetings on Sydney harbour, whereat
black male and female missionaries danced the voluptuous
dances of their nation and.wreathed their oily locks with
gum-leaves—that the said copper-coloured brudders and
sistahs are forbidden to indulge in high-kicking exercises
in their own islands under the careful eye of their par-
ticular Day. and Martin potentate who gives his cogent
(and unprintable) reasons in full on the sheet of bark that
constitutes the local statutes. But, of.course, the single-
minded Chadbands of civilisation can see no harm in it.
By the way, these happy natives were invited to tea by a
suburban millionaire Methodist, who gathered a swarm of
parsons and parsons’ wives to help their coloured bretheren
to enjoy themselves. .But one cleric’s good lady left the
festivities abruptl} - in a huff because one of those diead-
ful black women actually pulled up her stocking right in
front of Brother Blank.”
A Sydney suburban.medico tells a yarn (cn parenthese,
I rather distrust the genuineness of doctors’ original stories
—don’t you? - I think they lay them in with the red-lamp
and the lancet, and the rest of the.stock-in-tradelabout
his wife who was dangerously ill, and even “ given up.”
One evening her husband was smoothing.her pillow fat-
her when she suddenly opened her eyes and said faintly ;
“I don’t think I shall he much longer with you, George,
and I’ve been thinking that when I am gone you couldn’t
do better than marry Mrs. Dodson!” Quite a touching
instance of wifely self-sacrifice, Wasn’t It? Its usual
■effect is to make hubby perjure himself by vowing
never to let another woman usurp her place. But I
know one lady who fixed her glazy eye on- her lord and
master, and said in deep, sepulchral tones: “William,
you must never marry again. If you do, I’ll haunt you.”
Some of the stuffs they are • wearing now have
large lozenge-shaped dots all over them, and con-
sidering that—in heraldry—a lozenge means a very con-
siderable bar-sinster, it looks rather blushable to see the
society girl perambulating in a garment covered with
lozenges from collar to flounce. More so, since we are
told that it is the caper to wear one’s coat-of-arms broidered
■on one’s outer garments. Can there be a deep, dark,
family reason for this society outbreak of lozenge-spots ?

SAPPHO SMITH.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
On looking over the history of the past SO
Melbourne Cups we find that over 1000 horses have
sported silk for that great event. The V.11.C. has given
away in Cup-money alone £65,000. Out of 30 winners
three have been aged ; five, 6yrs. ; nine, syrs. ; six, 4yvs. ;•
seven, Syrs. In colour they have been—l 7 boy, 9
brown, 2 black, 1 grey and 1 chestnut. Four were geldings,
presumably ending their days as cab-horses. A marc, and
only one, ever got her nose in front at the post, and she
was a clinker. Victoria lays claim to 15 Cups ; N.5.W.,-
13; S. A., 1; Tasmania, 1. As yet none of the N.Z.
gentlemen have had the good luck to annex a
Cup, but it will happen some day. The yellovv-
and-black and all-black have each scored four in honour of
N.S.W, The largest field to face the flag was in Sheet
Anchor's year—3s against 7in Banker’s. Carbine left his
field behind him with a crusher of lOst. 51b. ; Banker did
the same with the feather sst. 91b. Tire double winner,
Archer, look 3min. 52sec. to get home ; last year’s great-
gun travelled the distance in Bmin. 28jsecs., hut in Archer’s
day the turf was not velvet as it is now. The lamb has
backed the favourite six times and got home ; 24 times ho
has lost his wool. Cults, Pigott, and O’Brien have had
the good fortune to steer the winner twice each. The
lowest amount run for was in 1863, when the race was
worth a little over £5OO ; the highest, £lo,ooo—for last
year's spin. The best horse to pass the post, without
doubt, was Carbine ; the worst, that miserable three-
legged brute, Zulu —and yet he was a near relation of the
once mighty Barb.
# # *
The greatest white elephant ever presented to
man or boy is the massive gold whip so kindly and regu-
larly given year after year by hotel-keeper Reynolds to
the winning mount of the Melbourne Cup. As a flagella-
tor it is useless, and no one expects the travelling jock, to
carry a safe about with him to carry the-toy in. Cannot
some kind friend knock it into Mr. R.’s head that a stop-
watch is the jockeys best friend ?
# * #
Trank Mahony’s picture of Bungebah is, in the
writer’s opinion, the best specimen of racehorse por-
traiture he has seen in Australia. The picture was lately
on view in a George-street (Sydney) window.
* # #
An ill-informed twaddler for Melbourne Herald
predicts that the Epsom Derby will “yield the palm to
one of the great handicaps, such as the City and
Suburban.” Rubbish ! The City and Sub. has been
declining a good deal faster than the Derby for some
years past. The Derby will always be a popular three-
year-old race, because it happens at the right time, when
all the racing “ swells” are in town and the form of the
horses has not been fairly exposed. For both reasons
it will always take precedence of the Leger.
# # *
Racing is likely to go ahead in South Australia
where the poor hacker gets a little encouragement to per-
severe. One winner, Syntax, at the A.R.C. meeting,
returned a totalisator div. of £72 per sovereign. There
were thirteen runners, and the “books” with their im-
pudent bawl of “H’any price outsiders!” wouldn’t lay
more than 10 to 1 Syntax. The machine will gradually
teach the Australians what an “outsider” means, a point
on which they and their Hebrew despoilers are quite
pathetically ignorant. In 1872 four horses started for the
Goodwood Cup —Favonius, Mortefner, Shannon and
another. The heavy wagering was all between the two
first-named horses, a shade of odds being laid on one
and a slightly lesser shade of odds against the other.
Against Shannon some of the ringmen offered 66 to 1 or
100 to 1, until they had booked a bet or two at the longer
price, and that real good mare won in a canter to the joy
of the layers. If Carbine, Marvel, Highborn, and The
Admiral were to engage in a two-mile race in England
at weight-for-age, the big ringmen would lay (if there were
anything of a market) at leastso-to 1 against either of the
two “ outsiders.” At Randwick or Fleming ton you might
get 7to 1 Highborn and 10 to 1 The Admiral. If yon ex-
postulated with the Children of Israel t hey would shriek
—“ Didnlt Highborn win the Sydney Cup, didn’t we see
The Admiral win the Derby—what'are yer givin’ us?”
Which wouldn’t be all bluff, either. The Jew bookmaker
is not necessarily a genius, and, so long as the public
remain hopeless idiots on the sub j yet of racing odds, he
needn’t cultivate the art of betting “ close to figures.”

The Referee last to hand, April 20, contains
the following, by “ Pen dragon,” to which a mournful in-
terest attaches in view of the fact that that world-famous
writer has since died “ Otherwise—and I will say it, in
spite of the stern and inflexible one who is going to take
his penny to another shop if I tell about hunting—
otherwise, and bating the nine weeks, of frost and snow,
I have had a very g’ood time indeed this winter. Every
now and again I have been'so frightened that business'
and other troubles and worries, which seemed as if they
must, be always with me, have Ijetn entirely forgotten.
As they say—they who know most about it—if you have
lots of flight you won’t want much physic ;‘and this may
1)3 the reason why thy health is so much better than it
was at the beginning of the season. If there he any one
among you who See this that thinks nothing will make
him shake off his shop cares and his homo annoyances,
let him take my tip-buy a horse and go a-lmnting.
Then, mayhap, he’ll have other tilings to think of!
Reader, the delights of hunting are many and various.
I’ll tell you how comes what I think is the very best of
them. That is when the ruff is over, and you find youiv
self still alive, and whole ; you in such case feel quite
brave again ; -you turn your horse’s head homewards, eat
up your sandwiches, light your pipe or cigar, and take a
long and steady pull at your whisky and water.
* * #
The “Queen’s Birthday” meeting at Fleming-
ton was enlivened by a comedy in the paddock during
luncheon interval. The performers were the acting-
manager and trainer of a candidate for one of the forth-
coming events, and from as much as could bo heard of
the dialogue it appeared that they were “ all broke,” and
that if one didn’t let the other “ have a hundred of it”
the other would rush off and do something alarming. A
good deal of gory matter was introduced into the conver-
sation, and finally one of them made a hysterical exit
with his colleague in pursuit. As the horse didn’t win, it
may be charitably supposed that they became more
broke than ever.

Money seems to be more plentiful than ever with
the English “ ring.” The winner of a Two-year-old Plate
at Sundown Park the other week was backed to win
£20,000 without diminishing the odds on offer. The
commissioner started by accepting a bet of 1000 to 120,
and, after making a round of tire hooks, was able to come
back to the first layer and get a little bit more on at ih -
old price. Out here the rule is to reduce the odds by one-
half after a small commission has been worked, and then,
if the public follow suit to any extent, the scared
Hebrews become ‘•full” and refuse to lay any pi ice at
all. There is some reason for this, however. The fact
of one horse being genuinely backed is strong presump-
tive evidence that 75 per cent, of the field is as stiff as a
duchess at a mixed gathering.

There’s a yarn going round on the subject of a
recent Jockey Club enquiry into the condition of the
British Turf. A fashionable light-weight, calkd upon to
explain why he had £30,000 at the bank, told the “most
exclusive club in Christendom ”to go to blazes. He
didn’t care a dash whether he went on riding or not, and
would be’appy to lend a thousand or two to any noble
lord who ’appened to be wuss broke than usual.

The Age cableman sends word that Tom Taylor
has made a break of 1407 in a spot-stroke-ftimed game.
If that’s true, then the pockets were bigger than the
regulation size. If everything was fair and straight, and
the points were actually made, still we may expect to
see Roberts (who doesn’t run up such breaks) give Tom
Taylor half the game and a licking. It was a queer item
of news to cable, anyway.

Sam Davis, now owner of the Palace Hotel,
Newmarket, Vic., was, in the old days, one of Australia’s
crack light-weights, and while wasting to ride John
Cleeland’s Barwon, sent his friend Sharpe to the chemist’s
for three-penn’orth (one ounce) of Epsom Salts. Sharpe,
knowing no better, went to the local Cheap Jack’s store,
got six ounces for the 3d, and mixed the lot, and the small
gentleman with the very large feet remarked as he
smacked his lips after the dose, “ that they were d—d
strong.” How S. 1). fell off (after winning) from weakness
is a matter of racing history.
The Rise and Shine G old-mining' Co.,
Ltd., Tooloom, N.S.W.
Tun Rise arid Shine Gold-mining Co., which was
formed to acquire an area of 16 acres of apparently
good gold-bearing country situated at Tooloom,
Clarence- district, N.S.W., and close to the
Queensland border, was registered in Brisbane as
a non-liability company on Saturday, the 10th
-May,. 1891. The capital of the company is
£30,000 in 60,000 shares,- 17,500 being fully paid
up, 35,000 being paid up to 95., and 7500 shares
being reserved for future sale. A report on the
mine has been made by Mr. H. Tre near (late
assayer and smelter at the Mount Morgan works).
He speaks very favourably of the venture, which
up to recently has been worked by Messrs., Payne
and Sons, the owners. He says that payable gold
is found throughout the reef, and that the general
character of the metal is coarse and flakey. In
closing his report he states: “After spending
three days at the mine and carefully
examining the lode and its contents I
have come to the conclusion that the pro-
perty is a valuable one and well worth the
attention of mining men. It is not a big reef,
but it is rich in gold, and when the present primi-
tive, laborious, and costly mode of working gives
place to a better system of mining, it ought to pay
handsomely.” Mr. Trenear further states that
the gold occurs chiefly in nodules of quartz, iron
oxide, and pink-coloured felspar on the hanging
wall, some of the stone, when broken, actually
hanging together with the precious metal. He
likewise mentions that there is no battery or
machine! on the field, and that the gold is obtained
by Messrs. Payne and Sons washing the stone
and “dollying” the specimens. By this means
they have mint account sales for over £l5OO for
gold sold. The present Company has been formed
in Brisbane, and the bulk of the proprietary shares
are held by residents in that city. An influential
directorate of well-known and wealthy colonists
is associated with the Company. At a meeting
of the directors held on the day of registration
the services of a competent and practical mining
manager was'engaged. It is the intention of the
proprietary to immediately arrange for a tunnel
to be driven into the hill to catch the reef, as sug-
gested by Mr. Trenear, and also to select a
machine-site on the river. The Rise and Shine
G.M. Co. is said to be one of the few good things
in recent mining developments. The prospectus
and report will be found in our advertising
columns,

A Latter-Day Lazarus.
(SPECIAL “BULLETIN” TRANSLATION.)
The death of M. Mathias, when it became
known in the little town of Lyre-sur-Ys, caused
a general shock of surprise. A man of barely 45,
robust, straight as an I, and who—there was the
pity of it—had only three years before married a
girl of 20, a charming little woman whom he loved
to distraction.
Naturally, M. Mathias, being dead, obtained a
rapid reputation for a lifetime adorned with all
the virtues. It would be a pretty thing to throw
up against him now, that he had been a usurer
and a skinflint ! Who would think of reviving
the rumour about his marriage, which was
not altogether to his credit, and which,
indeed, would have recalled the vague terror
inspired by the portly citizen, with his underhand
ways, wealthy and avaricious, spending his
leisure, they said, in compounding poisonous
drugs and experimenting with them on captured
dogs ? That was all a thing of the past. He was
dead—peace to his soul!
Besides, when they came to think over it, was
this death, after all, so unlooked-for? Evidently
M. Mathias had had presentiments. Had he not
quite lately rebuilt, with workmen brought all
the way from Paris, the family-vault that awaited
i i mortal remains ? Moreover, he had been in a
very restless mood for some time past. He had
prowled about his own house as if in search of
some mysteiious robber. He kept his wife in
seclusion, and shut himself up for weeks in that
laboratory whose chimney flamed all through the
night. “ Cerebral shock,” said Dr. Labarre, with
a wise air. “ Serous apoplexy.”
They gave M. Mathias a splendid funeral. A
third of the population accompanied him to his
last home, and several eyes grew moist as the
crypt in the funeral chapel received the great oak
coffin—a regular monument, in which two men of
his size might have slept at their ease.
On the way home everyone asked what would
become of the widow ?
# * *
Now, the truth is that M. Mathias was
not dead.
Two hours after the ceremony this is what
might have been observed, had there been any
observers there:
After a sharp sound, like the click of a lock, the
coffin opened like a wardrobe, M. Mathias drew
himself up into a sitting position, and stretched
like a man just awake. Through a grated opening
in the upper wall, a glimmer of light found its
way, M. Mathias rose tj his feet, and gently
rubbed his knees, which were a little stiff.
Altogether, he felt very well, very comfortable.
The narcotic he had taken, after measuring it
with scrupulous care, had produced precisely the
required effect. They had believed him dead;
he had been buried ; everything was perfect.
Long before, M. Mathias had taken all his
precautions. The bottom of his vault had been
very skilfully arranged. It contained a change of
clothes, a store of eatables, and a few bottles of
good w’ne, which, as you can imagine, were just
at the proper temperature. Nothing makes the
stomach feel more empty than a funeral, even
your own, so M. Mathias, seated comfortably on
Ids coffin, broke into a game pic and then drank
to the future.
It is time to tell why M. Mathias was there,
six feet under ground, by his own will.
Of course there was a woman at the bottom of
it. After forty years of bachelorhood, M.
Mathias, a chemist who had made a fortune out
of an anti-spasmodic pill, fell in love with charm-
ing Anne I’iedefer, niece of the receiver of taxes
at Lyre-sur-Ys. He promptly proposed to the
girl, who no less promptly refused, thereby heat-
ing his ardour to the infatuation of an imbecile —
that is to say, of a man of forty who lets himself
fall in love. Being by nature dishonest, he
managed to entangle the old receiver so cunningly
that at the end of a year the unfortunate man,
knowing that there was a shortage in the Govern-
ment cash, thought seriously of suicide. Then M.
Mathias appeared as a saviour, and made his
little conditions. The niece sacrificed herself for
the uncle, who had been a father to her, and that
notwithstanding the very tender relations that
bound her to a notary’s clerk in thenext village. An
unhappy viefim, Anne became Madame Mathias.
She had resigned herself to her fate, and
accepted all the consequences of the catastrophe,
but M. Mathias, doing himself justice, was con-
vinced that she detested him. From that to the
belief that she was deceiving him, as he deserved,
was only a step. The suspicion ripened into a
monomania. His wife never went out ; no one
visited her. What of that? M. Mathias accused
his own dullness. If he had failed to find proof
of his wife's infidelity it must be because ho was
not sharp enough.
Then this brilliant idea came into his head —to
take a sham journey—not to Versailles or Havre,
like the husbands of the comedies, but a much
longer journey, and one from which the return
would seem much more difficult.
And he would come back, very much alive, one
of these nights and surprise his unfaithful spouse.
He had allowed himself three days, and he
thought over it all with great satisfaction as lie
stretched himself again comfortably in his coffin.
# * *
The third day was just over. M. Mathias
was trembling with impatience. He waited till
the cemetery clock struck eleven. The moment
had come !
His plans had been carefully laid. The wall of
the cemetery bounded his property. He had a
complete suit of black, so that he might dress in the
character of a spectral chemist. His shroud he
would only wear in the cemetery, for the sake of
local colour. Once he had scaled the wall he
would go straight to his wife’s room. And
then— !
M. Mathias dressed himself, and when every-
thing was ready lie swung open the tombstone,
climbed into the chapel above, opened the door,
and stepped out, his shroud under his arm.
Under the trees, he unfolded the great white
sheet and tried to throw it round his shoulders.
But the folds were heavy. He had to try again.
“ Hold hard !” said a voice behind him. “I’ll
give you a hand.”
# • #
If you have ever been in a cemetery at
midnight, trying to fix your shroud, you will see
at once how disagreeable this interruption must
have been.
The speaker was the caretaker of the place-
old Grimbot, an odd character, very well-known
in the neighbouring taverns. He came up to M.
Mathias, peered into his face, and exclaimed :
“ What! you, Monsieur Mathias! Already!”
M. Mathias, not a little embarrassed, distorted
his face, with the idea that a sinister appearance
would soon free him from his unwelcome com-
panion. But not at all. Grimbot good-naturedly
took the shroud and arranged it in the most
becoming fashion.
“I have left my tomb—” commenced M.
Mathias, in a sepulchral voice.
“So I see,” interrupted Grimbot. “You arc
in more of a hurry than the rest of them.”
M. Mathias did not wait to listen. He was
moving off now with great strides, on the tips of
his toes, phantom-fashion,
Grimbot walked alongside, and continued :
“Yes, the others don’t as a rule begin all at
once. It generally takes a month or two.”
M. Mathias wheeled round suddenly, waving
his arms:
“ Away, sacrilegious wretch ! Away !”
“Come, come,” said Grimbot, in a paternal
tone. “ I won’t annoy you. You want to take a
stroll, like your friends below, but ”
M. Mathias, growing savage, went straight
ahead, and disdained any reply. He saw through
the gloom the gate of the cemetery. Like a wise
man, he had several louis in his pocket.
“ No more talk,” he said, sharply, holding out
a couple of gold coins. “ The key.”
Grimbot started back :
“ The key ! You want to go out! There's an
idea ! No, no ; you can’t ”
“Four louis,”groaned M. Mathias.
“Come, now,” rejoined Grimbot severely,
“none of that, you know. You may leave your
chapel, you may stretch your legs, and I have
nothing to say. The others leave their ”
‘‘ The others ! What others ?”
Grimbot gave a comprehensive wave of the
hand ;
“ Why, the dead.”
“ The dead ! Who is speaking of the dead? I
am alive!”
“ Oh, yes you will have your joke. But come
along ; I’m a decent fellow ; we’ll have a drink.”
His hand gripped like a vyce the wrist of M.
Mathias, whom he led forcibly to the little build-
ing where he lived, and thrust into a room on the
ground floor.
M. Mathias was literally stunned.
Grimbot shut the door, took a bottle from the
dresser, and, filling two glasses, lifted one to his
lips with ;
“Your health, Monsieur Mathias 1”
* * *
“Listen, my good fellow,” said M.
Mathias. “You want your joke. Very well.
But there is a time for everything. You know
quite well that I am alive. For private reasons,
X let myself be buried. But now I must get out,
on very serious business. I will pay you well,
trust me. ”
As he spoke, Grimbot had quietly moved round
tire table, and now he stood with his back against
t!ie door.
“ A pretty story,” he said, sarcastically. “ Ah,
so you arc alive ! You are not the first that has
told me that. I hear some very queer things.
You see, I am very fond of my guests. Every
night one or two of them come to take a friendly
glass. Last night, it was the notary —you remem-
ber Kadel, your neighbour—the one that has the
broken column. The night before, it was Madame
Claudin— a fine woman that! I’m a decent chap,
I let them take the night air, we have a little chat
—but lee them out!—that would be a nice
thing!”
M. Mathias grew haggard. Grimbot was
speaking with perfect sang /void like a
responsible functionary.
He was of middle height, thick-set, with the
hands of a gorilla. His eyes were black, glitter-
ing. M. Mathias shuddered. The man was
mad !
Yes, that was it. He had visions. And —
sign of hopeless insanity !—he heard the voices of
the dead. He peopled the cemetery with ghosts.
He lived in a fantastic world, the creature of
his drunken imagination.
M. Mathias began coaxing, pleading* promising,
beseeching. “What! the good, the intelligent
Grimbot could really take him for a dead man !
It was too amusing. Ha ! ha !”
“That will do,” broke in Grimbot, gruffly.
“ You won't be sensible. Time to go back.”
“Back? Where?”
“ To your own place—corner of the third row.”
“To the vault? Never !”
“You won’t! Once ! twice !—”
M. Mathias saw the enormous hands tremble,
lie grew sick, ho looked round for a way of
escape. There was only one—the door, and but-
tressed against it Grimbot. So much the worse.
He must get past at any cost. With a shout he
rushed forward.
Grimbot calmly put out his open hand and his
aggressor’s throat fitted into it. M. Mathias
gasped and tried to struggle. The claw tightened.
M. Mathias hung suspended at the end of an
arm. He kicked convulsively once or twice, then
a shudder, and he was still.
Grimbot, who had seen many such before, threw
him over his shoulder and carried him off with the
slow, dignified step of a trusty keeper to the
chapel, tossed him into the vault, swung the stone
into place with a kick, shut the grating, and
resumed his walk amongst the tombs, grumbling
to himself—
“ A pretty cheek ! Go out! And my billet!”—
* * •
That was how the widow of M. Mathias
was able to marry the man she had always loved.

MERELY THE TRUTH.
They have merit.
The fact is indisputable.
The proof lies in the free samples.
It is not enough to say they sometimes fail.
They are of human produce, and therefore fallible.
The real facts are—
Do they cure diseases of the blood and skin in the majority of in-
stances ? Undoubtedly they do.
Do they undo the effect of a chill or congestion, by bringing about
normal conditions of health ? Yes, they loosen and disperse the congestion.
Do they permeate the blood aseptically, so that bacilli and gcsuns
cannot multiply and the growth of disease is checked ? Yes, they do.
Do they confer relief, assuage pain, and benefit the general health,
even when they do not cure ? Invariably, yes.
Do they act as a natural healthy laxative, rendering them especially
valuable to women and children, and to men of sedentary habits of life or
business ? Yes, by tonic action only.
Do they free the inner skin pores and intricacies from taint, clearing
off Eruptions and improving the complexion ? They do.
Does their palatable taste and safety render them pre-eminently a
family medicine ? Yes, for neither the mind nor stomach rebels against
them, and children like them.
Are they the best of all blood purifiers and the most valuable of all
spring cleansing medicines ? Yes, they are.

“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at Sydney, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia's Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
Payment of Members.
One Person, one Yote.
State Revenue derived directly from the Land.
Complete Secularisation of State Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
The Bulletin denounces—
Religious Interference in Politics,
Foreign Titles.
The Chinese.
Imperial Federation.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more 1
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is foundi
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic'
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started eleven years ago with no capital but brains,
?r.u has become a vast property, because it pos.
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists'
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductons claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has.
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerfull
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-,
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and!
weekly press prior to and immediately after the'
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural 1
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some’’
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings;
with the latest appliances for high-class printing:
and the production of illustrations, and they have'
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned withi
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, thi'oughout its career, has;
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among:
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress : it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afllicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Ranges, A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed bo broadly based
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact —
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently ren ailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quote d in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies. '
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest ami most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

cations to be addressed to J. F. Archibald.

10th December, 188 S.
To 11. F. KUGEUiANK, Esq.
Dear Sir,- This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. 1 consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, but I became worse every day, wasting away until
1 became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains iu my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was cxpectoratingcverv day, 1 became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill 1 consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
end of three months I was taken had one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. Front that time I commenced to~ improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty's
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, la the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
Peter Smvtiie, J.P.
